# 124. The lexicon of Proto-Indo-European

1.Basic vocabulary items

2.The human being

3.Society

4.Technology

5.Nature

6.Abstract concepts

7.Religion

9.Vocabulary List

10.References

## 0. Methodological questions

The reconstruction of the lexicon of a proto-language is fraught with a number of problems and uncertainties. In the best case, the data in the individual languages correspond exactly and thus allow for the reconstruction of a common form ancestral to those of the daughter languages. But there are no hard and fast rules for sufficient criteria apart from the phonological correspondence. The probability of dealing with an inherited form increases with the number of languages independently attesting the form in question (cf. Meillet’s rule of thumb of three languages and Kretschmer’s “Randsprachenarchaismen”) and with the decreasing productivity of the word-formation process in question: if the latter is still productive in the daughter languages, the form might well have arisen independently. The ontological status of reconstructed forms is disputed − they are seen either as pure abstractions and shorthand notations for the attested forms or as more or less reliable approximations to actual language data.

In addition to the question of whether a form is inherited from the proto-language, there is further uncertainty as regards the <i>signifié</i>: “identical” meanings in the daughter languages may be due to contact or be independent developments. Even if an etymology seems impeccable in form and content, it may be as fallacious as a Proto-Norse term for ‘stamp’ which one might reconstruct on the basis of Swed. <i>frimärke</i>, Norw. <i>frimerke</i> and Dan. <i>frimærke</i> (cf. Seebold 1981: 48 f.). The uncertainty increases in those cases where the lexical items are not the same in the daughter languages. There does not seem to be a definite answer to the question of whether it is possible to reconstruct an inherited content without an inherited form. A case in point is the syntagm ‘dark earth’ claimed for PIE on the basis of OIr. <i>domun donn</i>, Hitt. <i>dankuiaz tagnaz</i> (abl.), Serb. <i>crna zemlja</i> and Gk. <i>gaĩa</i> / <i>Dēmḗtēr mélaina</i>/<i>kʰtʰṑn kelainḗ</i>, which according to Campanile (1987: 22) has “a greater probability than any Aryo-Greek isogloss”, but which is most probably a calque from Hurrian in Hittite borrowed into Greek and which may have arisen independently in the western languages (cf. Oettinger 1989/90).

While in a case like *<i>h₂ek̑</i>-<i>me</i>/<i>on</i>- meaning ‘stone’ in some daughter languages and ‘sky’ in others (or having both meanings in one and the same language, cf. Skt. <i>aśman</i>-‘stone’, probably ‘sky’, Av. <i>asman</i>- ‘stone; sky’, Gk. <i>ákmōn</i> ‘anvil’, ‘sky’ in <i>ákmōn</i> … <i>ouranós</i> [Hsch.] [156]) one may assume that speakers of PIE conceptualized the sky as made of stone, the solution is less evident in cases like Lat. <i>fāgus</i> ‘beech’ vs. Gk. <i>pʰēgós</i> ‘oak’ [287], where the divergence of attested meanings makes it difficult to assess the situation in the proto-language.

In dealing with these problems, different solutions have been offered in the past, with one stream of researchers clinging to phonological and semantic equations as the only basis for reconstructing lexical items for the proto-language and being dismissive about the possibility of making any solid statements about the “culture” of the speakers of PIE. The opposite direction, which one could call “cultural reconstruction” (Campanile 1987: 23), uses the term “semantic reconstruction” (cf. also Wachter 1998) and aims at comparing different IE traditions and extracting the elements common to them, starting with cases where both form and meaning match, but then leaving the area of strict word equations and assuming that the same inherited content may be expressed differently in different IE traditions. Cf. for the latter tradition Benveniste (1969.2: 179): “Si en effet on se borne à considérer la portion du vocabulaire qui peut être définie complètement et immédiatement par des correspondances régulières, on est condamné à voir peu à peu l’objet de l’étude se dissoudre.” Benveniste took a rather cautious position by studying cultural terms first within each language, insofar as they can be subjected to etymology. The results of these individual studies may coincide, but this alone is still not proof of their common inheritance: if several IE languages express the content ‘servant/to serve’ by words meaning ‘run/go around s.b.‘(*<i>peri</i>-, *<i>h₂m̥bʰi</i>- + *<i>kᵘ̯</i>elh₁-, *<i>h₂eg̑</i>-, *<i>ret</i>-), without there being a form common to all languages, is it possible to assume that PIE also had a word for ‘servant’ built from this or similar material or are these independent innovations in each language?

<!-- source-file: content/14_chapter08_11.xhtml -->

It is with these caveats in mind that the following material has to be assessed. In many cases the exact meaning of the proto-lexeme seems beyond the reach of reconstruction, in others the form is attested only in some sub-branches making its very existence in the proto-language questionable.

The first part of the material given in the appendix is arranged according to the extended Swadesh list (nos. 27−207).

## 1. Basic vocabulary items

Basic verbs that can be reconstructed for the proto-language include a copula and <i>verbum existentiae</i> *<i>h₁es</i>- [208] that in many languages is in suppletion with *<i>bʰuh2-</i> ‘grow, become, be’ [209], verbs denoting body posture such as *<i>legʰ</i>-/<i>ei̯</i>- ‘lie (down)’ [123], *<i>sed</i>-/<i>h₁ēs</i>- ‘sit (down)’ [124], and *<i>steh₂-</i> ‘(take a) stand’ [125] and body movement such as *<i>h₁ei̯</i>-, *<i>gᵘ̯em</i>- ‘go/come’ [121], *<i>bʰerg̑ʰ</i>-, *<i>h₁rei̯</i>- ‘rise’ [210], *<i>sekᵘ̯</i>- ‘follow’ [212], *<i>bʰegᵘ̯</i>-, *<i>drem</i>- (and others) ‘run’ [215] *<i>bʰeu̯g</i>- ‘escape’, *<i>nes</i>- ‘escape, come home’ [216], *<i>h₁er</i>-, *<i>h₂nek̑</i>-, *<i>sei̯k</i>- ‘reach’ [213], verbs of ingestion such as *<i>h₁ed</i>-‘eat’ [93], *<i>peh₃</i>- ‘drink’ [92], *<i>kᵘ̯em</i>- and *<i>srebʰ</i>- ‘sip’ [219], *<i>gᵘ̯</i>erh₃- ‘devour’ [222], *<i>su̯el</i>-, *<i>sleu̯g</i>-, etc. ‘swallow’ [218], and verbs for ‘sleeping’ and ‘waking’ like *<i>su̯ep</i>-, *<i>ses</i>- [107] and *<i>bʰeu̯dʰ</i>-, *<i>h₁ger</i>- [217] respectively. Apart from the problem of a seeming synonymy frequently beyond the reach of reconstruction, in some cases PIE seems to have had two verbs differing only in aspect/aktionsart, one expressing a state, the other the process leading up to that state (e.g., ‘sit’ vs. ‘sit down’ and ‘sleep’ vs. ‘fall asleep’), while in others various stem formations of the same verb expressed these differences (e.g., in the case of ‘stand/take a stand’ *<i>steh₂-</i> [125]). There is a larger number of verbs for ‘see’ [101] with different connotations (e.g., *<i>u̯ei̯d</i>- with that of knowledge, cf. the pf. *<i>u̯oi̯dh₂e</i> ‘I have seen, hence I know’, *<i>derk̑</i>- with that of the eyes emitting rays of light, cf. Gk. <i>drákōn</i> ‘snake, dragon’, originally ‘the one with flashing eyes’, Pi. <i>N</i>. 3.84 <i>dédorken pʰáos</i> ‘the light flashed’, *<i>spek̑</i>- that of ‘spying’, cf. Skt. <i>spaś</i>- [n.] ‘spy’, *<i>ser</i>- that of ‘watching over, guarding’) than for ‘hear’ and other verbs of perception. A safely reconstructible form for ‘hear’ is *<i>leu̯</i>- [102] with a derivative *<i>leu̯s</i>-, for ‘taste’ *<i>g̑eu̯s</i>- [221], for ‘smell’ *<i>h₃ed</i>- [105], while a convincing etymon for ‘feel/ touch’ is missing. Besides verbs for ‘bringing, carrying’ (*<i>bʰer</i>- [223]) and ‘putting’ such as *<i>dʰ</i>eh₁- and *<i>stel</i>- [224], there is a large number of verbs for ‘giving’ and ‘taking’ [128], some of which have both meanings in different languages respectively or even in the same language, e.g., *<i>nem</i>- is ‘take’ in Germanic but ‘give, distribute’ in Greek, *<i>deh₃-</i> is ‘take’ in Hittite, ‘give’ in most other languages, and in Sanskrit ‘give’ in the active and ‘take’ in the middle inflection, *<i>gʰebʰ</i>- is ‘take’ in Celtic, but ‘give’ in Germanic. The basic social activities of ‘sharing’ (*<i>bʰag</i>-, *<i>deh₂</i>- [225]) and ‘exchanging’ (*<i>mei̯</i>-, *<i>mei̯th₂</i>- [226]) seem to have played an important role not only in relationships between humans, but between humans and gods as well (cf. Av. <i>baγa-</i> ‘god’ < ‘distributor’ or ‘who gets a share [in the sacrifice]’, Gk. <i>Od</i>. 8.325 <i>tʰeoí</i>, <i>dōtêres heáōn</i> ‘the gods, the givers of goods’, cf. 8).

Many adjectives reconstructible for the proto-language can be analyzed as derivatives of other word classes, mostly verbs, e.g., *<i>gᵘ̯ʰer</i>-<i>mo</i>- ‘warm’ from *<i>gᵘ̯ʰer</i>- ‘be/become warm’ [180], *<i>h₁es</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘good’ [185] if from *<i>h₁es</i>- ‘be’ [208], *<i>mei̯H</i>-<i>u</i>- [32] ‘small’ from *<i>mei̯H</i>- ‘diminish’ [400], but also nouns, e.g., *<i>ped(i)i̯o</i>- ‘on foot, foot-’ [274] from *<i>ped</i>/<i>pod</i>- ‘foot’ [80], and even adverbs, e.g., *<i>neu̯o</i>- ‘new’ [183] if from *<i>nu</i> ‘now’, while a smaller group seems to establish a class of primary adjectives, e.g., *<i>sen</i>- ‘old’ [184] and *<i>meg̑</i>- ‘big’ [27]. Adjectives for basic physical properties that can be reconstructed with varying degrees of certainty are, among others, *<i>gᵘ̯(e)rh₂</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘heavy’ [31] and *<i>h₁le(n)gᵘ̯ʰ</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘light’ [265], *<i>ten(H)</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘thin’ [35], *<i>teg</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘thick’ and *<i>bʰn̥g̑ ʰ</i>-<i>u</i>-‘dense, thick’ [30], *<i>bʰr̥g̑ʰ</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘high’ [266], *<i>dʰeu̯b</i>- ‘deep’ [267], *<i>pl̥h₁</i>-<i>no</i>- ‘full’ [182] and *<i>h₁u̯(e)h₂</i>-<i>no</i>- ‘empty’ [270], *<i>Hōk̑-ú-</i> ‘fast’ [272], probably *<i>gᵘ̯r̥d</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘slow’ [273], *<i>h₂ek̑-ro</i>- ‘sharp’ [191] *<i>h₂sou̯so-</i> ‘dry’ [195], *<i>pl̥th₂</i>-<i>u</i>- and *<i>h₁u̯erH</i>-<i>u</i>- ‘broad, wide’ [275] and *<i>h₂emg̑ʰ</i>-<i>ú</i>- ‘narrow’ [34], *<i>medʰ-i̯o</i>- ‘middle’ [388], and various forms meaning ‘long’, *<i>dl̥h₁gʰ</i>-<i>o</i>-, *<i>mh₂-ro</i>-, *<i>duh₂-ro</i>- [28] and *<i>mreg̑ʰ</i>- ‘short’ [33].

Candidates for PIE color terms are *<i>kr̥sno-</i> ‘black’ [176] and *<i>albʰo</i>- ‘white’ [175] (while many other adjs. meaning ‘white’ also mean ‘shining’, cf. [175]), *<i>h₁rou̯dʰ- o</i>-/<i>h₁rudʰ-ro-</i> ‘red’ [172], *<i>g̑ʰl̥h</i>3- (with various suffixes) ‘green/yellow’ [173], *<i>bʰru</i>-<i>(no)</i>-, *<i>bʰer</i>-<i>o</i>- ‘brown’ [269], *<i>as</i>- and *<i>poli-</i> ‘grey’ [268].

## 2. The human being

The basic terms for ‘male adult person’ and ‘female adult person’ in PIE were probably *<i>h₂nē´r</i> ([37], possibly originating from the more abstract meaning of ‘strength, vital energy’ [cf. Hitt. <i>innara</i>- ‘having *<i>h₂ner</i>- inside’, Gk. <i>nō´ropi kʰalkō´i</i> ‘with bright bronze’]) and *<i>gᵘ̯en</i>-<i>(h₂</i>) [36], respectively. Other words for ‘man’ found in IE languages are *<i>man</i>-<i>u</i>- [291] and *<i>u̯iH</i>-<i>ro</i>- [37], originally an adj. ‘young, powerful, fresh’ (: Toch. A <i>wir</i> ‘young, fresh’, perhaps Alb. <i>ri</i> ‘young’, if with metathesis from *<i>u̯riH</i>-<i>o</i>-), frequently also ‘hero’. General terms for ‘male’ were *(<i>h₁</i>)<i>r̥sen</i>- [392] and *<i>u̯ers</i>- [396]. A different word for ‘woman’, *(<i>h₁e</i>)<i>sor</i>-, probably older than *<i>gᵘ̯en</i>-, has been suspected in forms like cLuv. <i>ašrulāhit</i>- ‘femininity’, Hitt. compounds like <i>ḫaššu</i>-<i>ššara</i>- ‘queen’ (derived from <i>ḫaššu</i>- ‘king’), the fem. forms of the numerals ‘three’ and ‘four’ in Indo-Iranian (: Skt. <i>tisrás</i>, <i>cátasras</i>, Av. <i>tišrō</i>, <i>cataŋrō</i>) and OIr. (: <i>téoir</i>, <i>cethéoir</i>), probably in the PIE word for ‘sister’, *<i>su̯esor</i>- [395], denoting the ‘female person of the same clan’ (if not from **<i>su̯e-h₁esh₂r</i>/<i>n</i>- ‘of the same blood’), and in Lat. <i>uxor</i> (if not orig. a fem. from *<i>h₂uks-en</i>- ‘bull, ox’ [313]) and even the name of the Greek goddess Hera (Willi 2010). Words for ‘old man/woman’ and ‘young man’ are derivatives of the respective adj., *<i>g̑erh₂-ont</i>- ‘old man’ [276], *<i>g̑erh₂-u<b>-</b></i> ‘old woman’ [277] (all from the verbal root *<i>g̑erh₂-</i> ‘rub, grind, make old’ [252]), *<i>h₂i̯u</i>-<i>Hen</i>- ‘young, youthful, young man’ [278] (probably originally ‘having youthful vigor / life-force’, *<i>h₂i̯u-h₃en</i>-, cf. [108]) in addition to other less widely distributed forms like *<i>magʰus</i> ‘boy, young man’ [370] and *<i>meri̯o</i>- [371].

Verbal roots for ‘live’, ‘engender, give birth’ and ‘die’ were *<i>gᵘ̯</i>i̯eh₃- [108], *<i>tek̑</i>- and *<i>g̑enh₁</i>- [249], and *<i>mer</i>- and *<i>nek̑</i>- [109], respectively.

Speakers of IE languages viewed themselves as ‘mortal’ and ‘terrestrial’ as opposed to the immortal heavenly gods (cf. 7), hence we find terms for ‘human being’ like *<i>mr̥to</i>- ‘mortal’ (: Arm. <i>mard</i> ‘man’ < *<i>mr̥to</i>- [presupposing a negative form *<i>n̥</i>-<i>mr̥ to</i>-‘immortal’], Ved. <i>márta</i>-, all from *<i>mer</i>- ‘die’ [109]; Toch. B <i>enkwe</i>, A <i>onk</i> ‘man’ < *<i>n̥k̑u̯os</i> ‘mortal’, cf. Gk. <i>nékus</i> ‘corpse’, OIr. <i>éc</i> ‘death’ < *<i>n̥k̑u</i>-, Av. <i>nasu</i>- ‘corpse, carrion’) and *<i>dʰg̑ ʰom</i>-<i>i̯o</i>-/-<i>on</i>- ‘earthling’ (: OIr. <i>duine</i>; Lat. <i>homō</i>, Goth. <i>guma</i>) from *<i>dʰeg̑ʰom</i>- ‘earth’ [159]. The same idea is expressed in the Homeric phrase <i>Il</i>. 5.442 <i>atʰanátōn te tʰeõn kʰamaì erkʰoménōn t’ antʰrṓpōn</i> ‘of the immortal gods and of men who walk on earth’. Correspondingly, the gods were the *<i>n̥</i>-<i>mr̥tōs</i> ‘immortal ones’ (: Gk. <i>ámbrotoi</i>, Lat. <i>dī immortales</i>, etc.).

Terms for body parts and internal organs are attested in most IE languages and reconstructible for the proto-language. They include *<i>k̑er</i>- ‘head’ ([72], the same root used for ‘horn’ [68], cf. Nussbaum 1986), *<i>g̑enu</i>- ‘chin’ [285], *<i>h₁d(o)nt</i>- ‘tooth’ [77] (a derivative of *<i>h₁ed</i>- ‘eat, bite’ [93]), *<i>h₃ekᵘ̯</i>- ‘eye’ [74] (an archaic dual form *<i>h₃(e)kᵘ̯ⁱʰ₁</i> ‘two eyes’ is attested in several languages, Gk. <i>ósse</i>, OCS <i>oči</i>, Lith. <i>akì</i>, Arm. <i>ač‘-k‘</i> [plurale tantum]), *<i>h₂ou̯s</i>- ‘ear’ [73], *<i>nas</i>- ‘nose’ [75], *<i>h₃oh₁</i>-<i>(e)s-</i> ‘mouth’ [76], *<i>(h₃)bʰruH</i>- ‘eyebrow’ [279], *<i>d⁽ʰ⁾n̥g̑ ʰuH</i>- ‘tongue’ [78] (forms with <i>l</i>- [Arm., Lith., Lat.] may be influenced by *<i>lei̯g̑ʰ</i>- ‘lick’ [220]), *<i>mon</i>-<i>o</i>- ‘neck, throat’ [87], *<i>bʰeh2g̑ʰ</i>- <i>u</i>- ‘arm’ [282] and *<i>Hol</i>-<i>en</i>- ‘elbow’ [283], *<i>Homso</i>- ‘shoulder’ [283], *<i>g̑ʰes</i>-<i>r</i>- ‘hand’ [83], *<i>h₂ek̑s</i>- ‘armpit’ [324], *<i>h₃nebʰ</i>- ‘navel’ [290], *<i>g̑e</i>/<i>onu</i>- ‘knee’ [82], *<i>ped</i>/<i>pod</i>-‘foot’ [80], *<i>h₃est</i>- ‘bone’ [65], *<i>perk̑</i>- ‘rib’ [379], *<i>tu̯ek</i>- ‘skin’ [62], *<i>Horso</i>- ‘buttocks’ [385]. A word for ‘beard’ was *<i>smek̑ru</i>-, while *<i>bʰar(s)dʰo</i>- [381] is attested only in some western IE languages. ‘hair’ in general may have been designated by *<i>peu̯mos</i>- [71].

Internal organs include *<i>erd</i>- ‘heart’ [90], *<i>i̯ekᵘ̯r̥(t)</i> ‘liver’ [91], *<i>pleu̯-mon</i>- ‘lung(s)’ [281] (from the root *<i>pleu̯-</i> ‘float’, as the lungs are lighter than other body parts and float on water), and the ‘womb’, *<i>gᵘ̯elbʰ</i>-<i>u</i>- [372]. The word for ‘gall’, *<i>g̑ʰolo</i>-, probably denoted both the organ (gall bladder) and its secretion (bile) [280], which was named after its color (cf. [173]). The belief that gall causes anger led to the metaphorical use of *<i>g̑ʰolo</i>- as ‘wrath’ (: Gk. <i>kʰólos</i>, Germ. <i>gallig</i> ‘malicious’, etc.). A term for ‘blood’ was the archaic heteroclitic noun *<i>h₁esh₂</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- [64]. Designations for male sexual organs are *<i>pes-</i> ‘penis’ [326] and *<i>h₁org̑ʰi</i>- ‘testicle’ [325], for female sexual organs maybe *<i>gᵘ̯ʰiHbʰ</i>- [360]. Verbs for sexual intercourse were probably *<i>(h₃)i̯ebʰ</i>- [263, orig. metaphorical] and *<i>h₁erg̑ʰ</i>- [211, cf. also 325].

## 3. Society

The kinship terms reconstructible for the proto-language are usually interpreted as pointing towards a patriarchal, patrilineal, and patrilocal society, i.e. descent was reckoned by the male line and brides left their homes to live with the family of their husbands. The word for ‘father’ *<i>ph₂ter</i>- [43] probably designated the head of the household as in Lat. <i>pater familias</i> and in the designation of Zeus (<i>Il</i>. 1.544) as <i><u>patḕr</u> andrõn te tʰeõn te</i> ‘<u>father</u> of men and gods’, although he is neither the genetic father of the other Olympian gods nor of mankind in general. For the genetic father other forms like *<i>g̑enh₁</i>-<i>ter</i>- may have been used (: Lat. <i>genitor</i>, Gk. <i>genétōr</i>, Skt. <i>janitár</i>-). Like *<i>mātēr</i> ‘mother’ [42], it may be derived from baby-talk syllables (*<i>pa[pa]</i>, *<i>ma[ma]</i>) that were integrated into the grammatical system by adding the suffix -<i>ter</i>- found in other kinship terms. Other etymological proposals connect *<i>ph₂ter</i>- with *<i>peh₂</i>- ‘protect’ [401] and *<i>māter</i>- with a root *<i>meh₂</i>- ‘make ripe’ (: Lat. <i>māturus</i> ‘ripe, timely’, Hitt. <i>mēḫur</i>- ‘time’), cf. Tremblay (2003: 85 f.). The suffix -<i>ter</i>- has usually been interpreted as being identical to the one forming agent nouns of the type *<i>deh₃-ter</i>/<i>tor-</i> ‘giver’. Pinault (2007) identifies *<i>-ter-</i> with the oppositive suffix found in forms like Gk. <i>deksiterós</i> ‘right’, Lat. <i>dexter</i> ‘id.’, etc.

‘Son’ and ‘daughter’ were *<i>suH</i>-<i>nu</i>- or *<i>suH</i>-<i>i̯u</i>- [292] (probably from a root ‘to bear’, *<i>seu̯H</i>- [227]) and *<i>dʰugh2ter</i>- ([293], usually connected with *<i>dʰeu̯gʰ</i>- ‘make useful, prepare; give milk’ [240] whence either ‘female servant’ or ‘suckled one’ or ‘one who will suckle’, cf. Tremblay (2003: 86). There were words for ‘grandfather’ (*<i>Heu̯o</i>-[373]), ‘grandmother’ (*<i>h₂en</i>- [398]), and, derived from the former, the ‘maternal uncle’ (*<i>Heu̯on</i>- [374]). Correspondingly, the term for ‘grandson’, *<i>nepot</i>- [295], came to be used for ‘nephew’ in some languages.

The word for ‘brother’, *<i>bʰreh2ter</i>- [294], probably denoted not just those with the same father or mother, but anyone belonging to the same ‘clan’, cf. the Gk. <i>pʰrḗtēr</i> ‘member of a clan-like group’, Lat. <i>frāter</i> ‘brother’, but also ‘member of a religious <i>collegium</i>’ (e.g., the <i>fratres Arvales</i>), while ‘sister’, *<i>su̯esor</i>- [395], may have meant ‘female person of the same clan’ (cf. 2).

Terms for ‘in-laws’ are *<i>deh₂iu̯er</i>- ‘husband’s brother’ [296], *<i>Hi̯enh₂-ter</i>- ‘husband’s brother’s wife’ [297], *<i>snuso-</i> ‘daughter-in-law’ [393], *<i>su̯ek̑uro-</i>/<i>su̯ek̑ru</i>- ‘father/mother-in-law’ [394], *<i>g̑l̥H</i>- ‘husband’s sister’ [399], and probably derivatives from a root *<i>g̑emH</i>- meaning ‘son-in-law’ [384].

The terms for ‘husband’, ‘wife’, ‘bride’, and ‘bridegroom’ found in the IE languages all seem to have acquired their meanings secondarily (e.g., Lat. <i>marītus</i> ‘husband’ beside Skt. <i>márya-</i> ‘young man’). A term coming close to ‘marry’, seen from the bridegroom’s perspective, is *<i>u̯edʰ</i>- ‘lead (the bride)’ [242]. Similarly, *<i>g̑emH</i>- has been suspected to have meant ‘buy (i.e. the bride)’ [384].

The term for ‘household’ in general and ‘house’ in particular was *<i>dom(h₂)</i>-<i>s</i>, gen. *<i>dem(h₂)</i>-<i>s</i> [298], whose master was the *<i>dem(h₂)s poti-</i> ‘master of the house’ [300]. A word for ‘(wing of a) door’ can be reconstructed as *<i>dʰu̯or</i>/<i>dʰur</i>- [383], one for ‘door-post’ as *<i>k̑h₂ent(H)</i>- [386]. The next larger unit was probably the *<i>u̯ei̯</i>- ‘settlement’ [299].

A characteristic trait of PIE society was the idea of exchange and reciprocity: A gift entails a counter-gift, being a guest entails being a host both for a former host or any member of his clan (cf. the famous encounter of Glaukos and Diomedes in <i>Il</i>. 6.120− 236). In Indo-Iranian, this idea of mutual obligation was divinized as the Ved. <i>Mitra</i>-, Av. <i>Miθra</i>-, lit. ‘contract’, probably from the root *<i>mei̯</i>- ‘give in exchange’ [226] (cf. the discussion in Mayrhofer 2006: s. v.). The same relation obtained between god and man (cf. 7).

The stranger, *<i>gʰosti</i>- [301], could be viewed both as one who had the right of hospitality, hence ‘guest’ in Germanic (: Goth. <i>gasts</i>) and Slavonic (: OCS <i>gostь</i>, most probably borrowed from Germanic), and as one who presents a danger to society, hence Latin <i>hostis</i> ‘enemy’, but also <i>hospes</i> ‘host’ from *<i>gʰosti</i>-<i>poti</i>- ‘master of the guest’, cf. Benveniste (1969.1: 87−101).

## 4. Technology

The PIE people were agricultors as can be seen in inherited terms for ‘grain’ such as *<i>g̑r̥h₂</i>-<i>no-</i> [302], orig. ‘ground’, a verbal adjective built to the root *<i>g̑erh₂</i>- ‘grind’ (that might be identical with *<i>g̑erh₂</i>- ‘make/get old, wear down’ [252]), *<i>i̯eu̯o</i>- ‘corn, barley, spelt’ [303], *<i>puHro</i>- ‘wheat’ [304] (perhaps from *<i>peu̯H</i>- ‘purify’, Skt. <i>punā´ti</i>, <i>pávate</i>, i.e. that which is purified on the threshing floor), and *<i>dʰoh₁</i>neh₂- ‘corn, seed’ [382] (perhaps from *<i>dʰ</i>eh₁- ‘put’ [sc. into the ground]). Also attested, though with more limited distribution, are *<i>u̯rugʰi̯o</i>- ‘rye’ [306] and *<i>bʰar</i>-<i>es</i>- ‘barley’ [305].

Verbs for ‘grind, mill’ beside *<i>g̑erh₂</i>- are *<i>melh₂</i>- [264] and *<i>h₂leh₁</i>- [264]. ‘sow’ is *<i>seh₁</i>- [243], ‘cut, pluck, reap’ is *<i>(s)kerp</i>- [307] (a root from which words for ‘fruit’ [54], ‘harvest’ [308], and ‘sickle’ [309] are derived). ‘To plough’ is *<i>h₂erh₃-</i> [231], cf. the corresponding instrument noun *<i>h₂erh₃</i>-<i>tro</i>- ‘plough’ [310]. A team of oxen [313] was <i>yoked</i> together (*<i>i̯eu̯g</i>- [238]) with the *<i>i̯ugom</i> [239]).

In addition to husbandry, the PIE people also domesticated animals and practiced stock breeding. Domesticated animals were the horse, *<i>h₁ék̑u̯os</i> [311] (perhaps related to *<i>h₁ōk̑ú</i>- ‘fast’ [272], therefore literally ‘the fast one’), the dog, *<i>u̯ō´n</i> [47], the cow, *<i>gᵘ̯ō´u̯s</i> [312] and ox, *<i>h₂uks-en</i>- [313] (probably a derivative from *<i>h₂u̯eks</i>- ‘grow [up]’ [209]), the sheep, *<i>h₂ou̯i</i>- [314], the lamb, *<i>h₂égᵘ̯⁽ʰ⁾no</i>- [315], the goat (for which a variety of roots are attested: *<i>h₂eig̑</i>-, *<i>gʰai̯do</i>-, *<i>h₂eg̑o</i>-, *<i>h₁er</i>-, *<i>kapro</i>- [316]), the pig (: *<i>suH</i>- [317]) and its farrow, *<i>pórk̑o</i>- [318] (customarily derived from a verbal root *<i>perk̑</i>- ‘to furrow’ [319], as ‘the furrowing animal’, but *<i>perk̑</i>- ‘speckled’ [320] is equally possible), and the goose, *<i>g̑ʰans</i>- [375].

The general term for domesticating was *<i>demh₂</i>- [262] which might be connected with the root *<i>dem(h₂)-</i> ‘build’ [261] (and ‘house’ in nominal forms [298]), if *<i>demh₂-</i> can be understood as ‘domestication’, i.e. ‘accustoming to the house’ (cf. the recent discussion of both roots in Nikolaev 2010).

Stock was considered a measure of wealth, hence *<i>pek̑u</i>- [321] probably meant both ‘livestock, cattle’ and ‘riches, wealth’, cf. Lat. <i>pecus</i> ‘livestock’ beside Goth. <i>faihu</i> ‘possession, wealth’. For a development from the latter to the former meaning, one might think of NE <i>cattle</i> < Lat. <i>capitāle</i> ‘principal sum of money, possession’ (cf. Clackson 2007: 206 ff.). The connection of *<i>pek̑u</i>- with the verbal root *<i>pek̑</i>- ‘pluck, pick (esp. wool)’ [234], according to which *<i>pek̑u</i>- would have designated ‘the animal to pluck wool from’, is difficult to maintain by reconstruction alone, since *<i>pek̑u</i>- is not limited to ‘sheep’, but probably designated originally any ‘moving’ property (including slaves) and was later restricted to [+animate, -human] as seen in the frequent pairing *<i>pek̑u</i>-<i>u̯iHro</i>- found in several languages (OAv. <i>pasūš vīrə̄ng̣</i> [acc.pl.], Umbr. <i>ueiro pequo</i> ‘homines et pecudes’, cf. Watkins 1979).

The IE language family attests a number of terms for operating with food, e. g., *<i>pekᵘ̯</i>- [235], which denotes both ‘ripen (intr.)’ and ‘cook, make edible’ (cf. Janda 2000: 48− 49), cf. also the adj. *<i>(H)ōmo</i>- ‘raw’ [271] and the noun *<i>kreu̯h₂s</i>- ‘blood, raw meat’ [63], *<i>pei̯s</i>- ‘crush’ [236], *<i>dʰei̯g̑ʰ</i>- ‘mold, shape’ (e.g., bread or clay, cf. Gk. <i>teĩkʰos</i> ‘wall’ beside NE <i>dough</i>) [244]. Elements of the PIE diet were *<i>mēms(o)</i>- ‘meat’ [63], some sort of broth or soup, *<i>i̯ūs</i> [322], *<i>medʰu</i>- ‘mead’ [389], a drink sweetened with honey (*<i>melit</i>- [359]), and probably ‘wine’ (*<i>u̯oi̯no</i>- [390]), which is likely to be an early loanword in PIE.

There was a word for ‘wear, clothe’ *<i>u̯es</i>- [259] (denoting the state) with numerous derivatives for ‘garment, clothing’ (: Lat. <i>uestis</i>, Skt. <i>vasana</i>-, <i>vastra</i>-, Gk. <i>estʰḗs</i>, Goth. <i>wasti</i>, etc.) beside *<i>h₂eu̯H</i>- (denoting the process) [323]. Whether or not the latter meant ‘put on shoes’ already in PIE, as it does in some daughter languages, is questionable: no word for ‘shoe’ is reconstructible for the proto-language, but one for ‘gird/girdle’, *<i>i̯eh₃s</i>- [260], is widely attested. Among the terms for producing clothes we find *<i>sneh₁</i>- ‘spin’ [245], *<i>u̯ebʰ</i>- [251] and *<i>tek</i>- [250] ‘weave’, and *<i>si̯eu̯</i>- ‘sew’ [138].

The use of the wheel and wheeled transport was probably adopted only shortly before the break-up of the PIE language community. This has been deduced from the fact that most terms in this semantic field seem to be metaphoric usages of words with different basic meanings: The nave or hub of the wheel originally designated the navel (: *<i>h₃nebʰ</i>- [290]), the ‘axis’ *<i>h₂ek̑s</i>- the ‘axle, armpit’ [324], and the word for ‘wheel’ itself is a reduplicated form from the root meaning ‘turn’ (: *<i>kᵘ̯</i>elh₁- [126]): *<i>kᵘ̯e</i>-<i>kᵘ̯</i>lh₁-<i>o</i>- [327], a frequently repeated metaphor, cf. Gk. <i>trokʰós</i> ‘wheel’ from <i>trékʰō</i> ‘run’, originally ‘turn’ (cf. Létoublon/de Lamberterie 1980; note also Lat. <i>rota</i> ‘wheel’ from *<i>ret</i>- ‘run’ [215]), S.-Cr. <i>točak</i> ‘wheel’ from *<i>tekᵘ̯</i>- ‘run’ [215]. The widely attested verb for ‘drive, transport in a vehicle’ (: *<i>u̯eg̑ʰ</i>- [230]) probably meant ‘hover’ originally (cf. Schlerath 1996).

## 5. Nature

The deified bright sky in PIE was called *<i>di̯ḗu̯s</i> [162] (cf. 7). In some languages, the PIE word for ‘cloud’ *<i>nebʰos</i> (probably originally from a verbal root *<i>nebʰ</i>- ‘be wet, cloudy’ [160]) changed its meaning to ‘sky’ (cf. OCS <i>nebo</i>, Hitt. <i>nepiš</i>, Skt. <i>nabhas-</i>[post-RV]). The same idea of the ‘bright one’ is expressed in OIr. <i>erc</i> ‘sky’ from a verbal root *<i>h₁erkᵘ̯</i>- ‘shine’ [141].

Candidates for PIE words for ‘day’ beside *<i>di̯eu̯</i>- are *<i>(H)āmer</i>- [178] and *<i>h₂eg̑ʰ</i>- [178], both with limited distribution, for ‘morning’ *<i>h₂ei̯</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- [328] and for ‘night’ *<i>nokᵘ̯ts</i> [177], based on a verbal root that may be attested in Hitt. <i>nekuzzi</i> ‘becomes dark’. In various IE (and non-IE) traditions, the night is described as wearing a garment adorned with the shining stars (cf. Katz 2000). A term for ‘darkness’ was *<i>(h₁)regᵘ̯os</i> [329], in Greek denoting the ‘underworld’. The word for ‘dawn’ *<i>h₂eu̯s</i>-<i>os</i>- [330], also deified in PIE culture, was derived from *<i>h₂u̯es</i>- ‘shine’ [246], as are words for ‘east’ in some IE languages (e.g., Germ. *<i>austa-</i>, OE <i>ēast</i>, OHG <i>ōstan</i>, Av. <i>ušastara-</i>, Latv. <i>austrumi</i>), while ‘evening’ and ‘west’ [368] derive from *<i>u̯esp</i>- ‘enshroud, clothe’ [369] (cf. Katz 2000).

The fact that the word for ‘right’ also denotes ‘south’ (*<i>dek̑s</i>- [199]) indicates that the speakers of PIE oriented themselves facing east. Words for ‘left’ were frequently subject to replacement or distortion, as the left side was deemed unfortunate and defective (cf. Gk. <i>lordós</i> ‘bent backward [convex in front]’, Gael. <i>lorcach</i> ‘crooked’, MHG <i>lërz</i>, <i>lurz</i> ‘left’; Gk. <i>euō´numos</i> ‘left’, lit. ‘having a good name’ and <i>aristerós</i> ‘left’ from <i>áristos</i> ‘best’; a term for ‘left’ common to several languages is *<i>lai̯u̯os</i>, Gk. <i>laiós</i>, Lat. <i>laevus</i>, OCS <i>lěvъ</i>, probably Toch. B <i>laiwo</i> ‘lassitude’).

In addition to *<i>u̯et</i>-<i>es</i>- [179] other terms for ‘year’ seem to refer to the idea of time as cyclical movement, cf. *<i>h₂et</i>-<i>no</i>- [179] from *<i>h₂et</i>- ‘go’ (: Skt. <i>átati</i> ‘walks’ [121]), *<i>i̯ēr</i>- [179] probably from *<i>i̯eh₂-</i> ‘go, walk’ [121], hence *<i>i̯ēh₂</i>-<i>r</i>-, cf. also the Homeric formula (<i>Od</i>. 1.16, 7.261, 14.287) <i>all’ hóte dē étos ẽltʰe periploménōn eniautõn</i> (lit.) ‘but when (a) year of the revolving years had come’. The same idea may underlie toch. A <i>pᵘkäl</i>, B <i>pikul</i> ‘year’, if these forms derive from *<i>pi-kᵘ̯l̥</i>- corresponding to the Gk. epithet <i>epiplómenos</i> ‘revolving’ (cf. <i>periploménōn</i> above) said of <i>eniautós</i> ‘year’ (cf. Katz 2004). Words for seasons are *<i>g̑ʰei̯</i>- ‘winter’ [331], *<i>u̯es</i>-<i>r</i>- ‘spring’ [334], *<i>sem-</i>‘summer’ [332] and *<i>Hes</i>-<i>en</i>- ‘harvest time’ [333]. The lunar month was apparently used as a unit of time: the word for ‘month’ (PIE *<i>meh₁n̥s</i> [148]) is in some languages identical to that for ‘moon’, or a derivative of it (e.g., Germ. *<i>mēnōþ</i>- > NE <i>month</i>, NHG <i>Monat</i>). Both words go back to a root meaning ‘measure’ (PIE *<i>meh₁</i>- [248]). The word for ‘sun’ shows a highly archaic inflection combining a stem in -<i>l</i>- and in -<i>n</i>- in one paradigm, approximately *<i>seh₂u̯el</i>- vs. oblique *<i>s(e)h₂u̯en</i>- [147]. The common PIE word for ‘star’ was *<i>h₂ster</i>- [149], probably from the verbal root *<i>h₂eh₁s-</i> ‘burn, become dry’ [195] (cf. Pinault 2007).

Among terms for meteorological phenomena we find words for ‘snow’, *<i>snigᵘ̯ʰ</i>-<i>s</i> and *<i>snoi̯gᵘ̯ʰos</i> [335], which originally may have meant ‘sticky’, cf. Skt. <i>ásnihat</i> ‘stuck (lay wounded/dead)’, <i>snihyati</i> ‘sticks, becomes moist’ (cf. Hoffmann 1975−1992: 442−454), ‘rain’, *<i>h₂u̯ers</i>- [151], *<i>sh₂eu̯</i>- [376] (probably related to *<i>seu̯</i>- ‘press’ [228]), *<i>Hembʰ</i>- [377] (either an independent root or a derivative of *<i>nebʰ</i>- ‘be wet; cloud’ [160]), *<i>h₂u̯eh₁</i>-<i>nt</i>-/-<i>i̯u</i>-/-<i>i̯o</i>- ‘wind’ [163], derived from the verbal root ‘blow’ (: *<i>h₂u̯eh₁</i>-: [98]), and the verbal root *<i>(s)tenH</i>- ‘thunder’ [336].

The PIE word for ‘earth’ can be reconstructed as *<i>dʰeg̑ ʰom</i>- [159]. ‘Hills’ and ‘mountains’ were probably designated by forms such as *<i>gᵘ̯erH</i>- (probably the root ‘[be] heavy’) [31]) and *<i>bʰerg̑ʰ</i>- [171].

The absence of a common word for ‘sea’ (*<i>mori</i>- is attested only dialectally and may originally have meant ‘lake’ rather than ‘sea’ [154]) seems to indicate that the PIE people were inland settlers. Transportation on water was known to them, though, as evidenced by the widely attested *<i>neh₂u</i>- ‘ship’ [286] that was driven by oars (cf. *<i>h₁erh₁</i>- ‘row’ [229]). A word for ‘standing water, swamp’ is *<i>sel</i>-<i>es</i>-, found in Greek and Sanskrit [378].

The basic word for ‘tree’ and ‘wood’ was *<i>dor</i>-<i>u</i>- [51], from which the Germanic terms for ‘trust, loyalty’ are derived (cf. Goth. <i>triggwa</i> ‘alliance’, OHG <i>triuwa</i> ‘loyalty’, OE <i>trēow</i> ‘belief, loyalty, truth’, etc.). Various other words for specific trees are reconstructible, e.g., *<i>bʰeh2go</i>- ‘beech tree’ [287], *<i>bʰerHg̑</i>- ‘birch’ [342], *<i>perkᵘ̯us</i> ‘oak’ [343] and *<i>peu̯k̑</i>- ‘spruce, pine’ [391]. They have frequently been discussed in connection with the question of the PIE homeland, since their geographical distribution is limited. But it cannot be ruled out that this distribution has changed over time or that the words designated different trees originally. The reconstruction of terms for fruit trees is less secure, perhaps *<i>h₂ebel</i>- ‘apple’ [288], though only attested in northwestern IE languages, and *<i>kr̥no-</i> ‘cherry’ [289].

A metal known to speakers of PIE was *<i>h₂ei̯es</i>-, probably ‘bronze’ or ‘copper’ [337], which appears in archaeological finds from the early sixth millennium onwards, while the term for ‘iron’ varies across the languages, indicating its late appearance in most IE cultures (attested in archaeological finds from the fourth millennium onwards; *<i>īsarno</i>-‘iron’ in Germanic and Celtic [397] probably originally denoted the ‘holy’ metal, if from *<i>h₁isHro</i>-, cf. Gk. <i>hierós</i> ‘powerful, holy’, Skt. <i>iṣirá-</i>). A similar variation is found in words for ‘gold’, *<i>g̑ʰl̥to</i>- [344] (perhaps originally ‘yellow, shiny thing’ [173], derived from the root also found in the word for ‘gall’ [280]) besides *<i>h₂eu̯so</i>- [345], derived from the verbal root *<i>h₂u̯es-</i> ‘shine’ [246] found in the word for ‘dawn’ [330] as well. Similarly, the word for ‘silver’ is derived from a root *<i>h₂erg̑</i>- ‘white, shining’ [175] (: *<i>h₂r̥g̑[e]nto</i>- [338]).

PIE had two words for ‘fire’: animate *<i>Vgni</i>- [167], inanimate *<i>peh₂u̯er</i>/<i>n</i>- [167], and several for ‘water’, *<i>h₂ekᵘ̯</i>- [150] and *<i>h₂ep</i>- [387], inanimate *<i>u̯ed</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- [150], derived from a verbal root *<i>u̯ed</i>- ‘moisten, well’ [339], and *<i>u̯eh₁-</i> [150].

The generic term for ‘wild animal’ was *<i>g̑ ʰu̯ēr</i> [44], which may be derived from a verbal root *<i>g̑ʰu̯er</i>- ‘bend, walk with a hunch’ (: Skt. <i>hvárate</i>, YAv. <i>zbar</i>-, cf. Schindler 1972: 37 f.). Individual words for non-domesticated animals were frequently prone to tabooistic distortion or euphemistic designations. Both phenomena may be present in the word for ‘wolf’, PIE *<i>u̯l̥kᵘ̯os</i> (: Skt. <i>vŕ̥kaḥ</i>, Goth. <i>wulfs</i>), which has a variant form *<i>lukᵘ̯os</i> (: Gk. <i>lúkos</i>, Lat. <i>lupus</i>, a Sabellic loanword in Latin, cf. also *<i>u̯l̥pē</i>- in Lat. <i>vulpēs</i> ‘fox’) and may have been a euphemistic term ‘the dangerous one’ (in order not to utter the animal’s ‘real’ name), cf. Skt. (adj.) <i>a</i>-<i>vr̥ká-</i> ‘safe’ (‘not dangerous’; prob. Hitt. <i>walkuwa</i>-‘dangerous’, cf. Lehrman 1987).

Similarly, the word for ‘bear’, which probably was *<i>h₂ŕ̥tk̑o</i>- [340] in PIE, has been replaced in Germanic by ‘the brown one’ (OHG <i>bēr</i>, ON <i>bjǫrn</i>, OE <i>bera</i>, cf. Lith. <i>bė́ras</i> ‘brown’; an alternative proposal relates the term to the word for ‘wild animal’ *<i>g̑ ʰu̯er</i>-[44]), in Slavonic by ‘honey-eater’ (: Russ. <i>medved’</i>) and in Baltic simply by ‘licker’ (: Lith. <i>loky˜s</i>).

Other animals known to PIE people were the hare, *<i>as</i>-<i>o</i>- [341], probably named after its color (cf. Lat. <i>cānus</i> ‘grey’ from *<i>as</i>-<i>no</i>-, OE <i>hasu</i> ‘grey-brown’), the beaver, *<i>bʰi</i>/<i>e</i>-<i>bʰru</i>- [347] (cf. Skt. <i>babhrú</i>- ‘brown’), the mouse, *<i>mūs</i> [346] (probably derived from the root *<i>meu̯sH</i>- ‘steal’, Skt. <i>muṣṇā´ti</i>; in Hitt. replaced by <i>kapirt</i>- which could be *<i>kom</i>-<i>bʰer</i>-<i>t</i>- ‘who collects, assembles’, cf. also from *<i>bʰer</i>- [223] *<i>bʰōr</i> ‘thief’, Gk. φώρ, Lat. <i>fūr</i>, cf. Oettinger 1995), and the worm *<i>u̯ermi</i>/<i>o</i>- [50].

The generic term for ‘fish’ was *<i>dʰg̑ʰu</i>- [45], in the western area of IE we find *<i>pei̯sk</i>-[45]. Salmon (: *<i>lak̑s</i>- [354]) and eel (*<i>h₂engᵘ̯ʰi</i>- [49]) were known, the latter also meant ‘snake’, a meaning also found in the form *<i>He</i>/<i>ogᵘ̯ʰi</i>- which may be related [49].

The generic term for ‘bird’ was *<i>h₂eu̯i</i>- [46], the word for ‘egg’ [67] *<i>(H)ōu̯i̯o</i>-, which Schindler (1969) analyzed as *<i>Hō</i>-<i>Hu̯i̯o</i>-, i.e. ‘that which is at the bird’, but which might equally well be a ‘vr̥ddhi’-formation *<i>h₂ōu̯i̯</i>-<i>o</i>- meaning ‘belonging to the bird’. Birds known to speakers of PIE were the crane, *<i>gerH-</i> [348], the eagle, *<i>h₂er</i>-<i>en</i>- [349], the thrush, *<i>trozdo-</i> [351], the sparrow, *<i>sper</i>- [350], the starling, *<i>storo-</i> [352] and the finch, *<i>(s)ping-</i> [353].

Names of insects reconstructible for PIE are ‘wasp’, *<i>u̯opseh₂-</i> [355], ‘hornet’, *<i>kr̥Hs</i>-<i>ro-</i> [356] (derived from the word for ‘horn’, PIE *<i>erh₂s-</i> [68], cf. Nussbaum 1986: 248−260), ‘fly’, *<i>mus-</i> [357], ‘louse, louse egg’, *<i>knid</i>- [48], ‘louse’, *<i>lūs</i>- [48], for the western languages ‘bee’, *<i>bʰei̯</i>- [358], while e.g., Greek and Armenian name the insect after its product *<i>melit</i>- ‘honey’ [359], cf. Gk. <i>mélitta</i> (<i>mélissa</i>) <*<i>melit</i>-<i>ih₂</i>, Arm. <i>mełow</i> from <i>mełr</i> ‘honey’.

## 6. Abstract concepts

Basic terms from the sphere of cognition were *<i>men</i>- ‘think’, *<i>mneh₂-</i>, a compound *<i>mn̥s</i>-<i>dʰ</i>eh₁- lit. ‘putting the mind (onto s.th.)’ from the same root [104], and *<i>g̑neh₃-</i> ‘recognize, know’ [103]. *<i>med</i>- [247] probably meant something like ‘take the appropriate measure (to establish/restore order)’ (as analyzed by Benveniste 1969.2: 123−132). Words for ‘learning’ were *<i>dens</i>- and *<i>h₁eu̯k</i>- [232], for ‘forgetting’ *<i>mers</i>- [233]. ‘govern, give directions’ was *<i>h₃reg̑</i>- [253] (orig. ‘stretch out [the hands]’, i.e. ‘give instructions, make straight’, cf. the Latin syntagm <i>regere fines</i> ‘to establish the borders of a sacred place’), from which the root noun *<i>h₃rēg̑</i>-<i>s</i> is derived, which in various languages denotes the ‘ruler, king’ [361] beside an <i>n</i>-stem *<i>h₃rēg̑</i>-<i>on</i>- (: Skt. <i>rāj</i>-<i>an</i>-; cf. Benveniste 1969.2: 9−15). The rules according to which governance proceeded was the *<i>i̯eu̯</i>-<i>s</i> ‘law’ (: Lat. <i>iūs</i>: probably originally ‘what is [up]right’, cf. Willi 2001), which was handed down orally from generation to generation, like all knowledge in a society without writing (cf. Lat. <i>fas</i> ‘[divine] law, custom’ < *‘what has been said’, from <i>fārī</i> ‘speak’, cf. Benveniste 1969.2: 133−142).

## 7. Religion

A PIE term for ‘believe, trust’ was the syntagm ‘put the heart’, *<i>red dʰ</i>eh₁- [362]. There was probably no general term for ‘pray’, but various verbs denoting specific types of interaction with the immortal gods such as asking (a favor) (*<i>prek̑</i>- [255]), entering into a reciprocal obligation (‘speak solemnly, vow’ *<i>h₁u̯egᵘ̯ʰ</i>- [254]) and praising (*<i>gᵘ̯erH</i>-[256], *<i>steu̯</i>- [237]). Different ways of presenting gifts to the gods are designated by roots such as *<i>spend</i>- ‘libate’ [258], which frequently takes on the meaning ‘vow, promise’ (cf. Gk. <i>spondḗ</i> ‘libation’, pl. <i>spondaí</i> ‘truce’ − the gods were witnesses and guarantors of the mutual obligations), *<i>g̑ʰeu̯</i>- ‘pour, libate’ [241], *<i>sep</i>- ‘attend to, worship’ [364] (with its derivative *<i>sep</i>-<i>el</i>- ‘honor’ in Skt. <i>saparyá</i>- ‘honor, sacrifice’ and Lat. <i>sepelīre</i>, -<i>iō</i> ‘bury’, originally ‘pay the last respects to s.b.’). The attitude towards the super-human may be one of both reverence and fear as expressed in *<i>Hi̯ag̑</i>- ‘fear, worship, sacrifice’ [257] and *<i>ti̯egᵘ̯</i>- ‘retreat, shy away from, feel awe’ [363].

There were various adjectives denoting the sphere of the ‘holy’ (cf. for a detailed analysis Benveniste 1969.2: 177−207), each with different connotations, e.g., *<i>sak</i>- ‘set apart from mankind’ [365], *<i>kai̯lo</i>- [366] ‘whole, healthy’, *<i>u̯ento</i>- ‘imbued with supernatural power’ [367] and similarly *<i>h₁isHro</i>- (: Gk. <i>hierós</i>, Skt. <i>iṣirá</i>-, cf. the common phrase <i>hieròn ménos</i>, Skt. [instr.] <i>iṣiréṇa mánasā</i> ‘inspired mind’, from *<i>h₁ei̯sh₂</i>- ‘give an impulse’, Skt. <i>iṣ-</i> ‘be lively, powerful; invigorate’, Gk. <i>iáomai</i> ‘I heal’, cf. García Ramón 1986).

The supreme god of the PIE pantheon was the deified bright sky (cf. 5), *<i>di̯ḗu̯s</i> (: Gk. <i>Zeús</i>, Skt. <i>d₍ᵢ₎yaúḥ</i>, Lat. <i>Iū</i>- in <i>Iūpiter</i>), derived from a root *<i>dei̯</i>- ‘shine’ [246], which also forms the basis of the general term for ‘god’, PIE *<i>dei̯u̯ós</i> ‘heavenly one’ [380]. A different idea of godliness seems to lie behind the root *<i>dʰeh1s-</i> appearing in Gk. <i>tʰeós</i> ‘god’, Lat. <i>fānum</i> ‘temple’ (*<i>dʰh₁snom</i>), <i>fēriae</i> ‘holi-days’, and Arm. <i>dik‘</i> ‘god(s)’, which is most probably related to *<i>dʰ</i>eh₁- ‘put, place’.

*<i>di̯ḗu̯s</i> was deemed responsible for various meteorological phenomena, cf. the Greek expressions <i>Zeùs húei</i>, <i>nípʰei</i> ‘Zeus rains, snows’ and nouns like Gk. <i>eudía</i> ‘good weather’ (*<i>h₁su</i>-<i>diu̯</i>-<i>eh₂</i>) and OCS <i>dъždь</i> ‘rain’ < *<i>dus</i>-<i>di̯u</i>-<i>s</i> ‘bad weather’. The personification is seen in the form of address ‘father’ (in the sense of ‘head of the household’ as in the Lat. <i>pater familias</i>, cf. 4) in the syntagm *<i>di̯ē´u̯s ph₂tē´r</i> ‘father sky’ (: Gk. [voc.] <i>Zeũ páter</i>, Skt. <i>dyaúṣ pitā´</i>, Lat. [originally voc.] <i>Iūpiter</i>, Umbr. <i>Iupater</i>). There are no good equations for a possible ‘wife’ of father sky, one proposal being a derivative of the same root, *<i>diu̯ōneh₂</i> (: Gk. <i>Di[w]ōnē</i>), cf. Dunkel (1991); however, a ‘daughter of the sky’ is usually reconstructed on the basis of expressions like Skt. <i>divó duhitā´</i>, Gk. <i>tʰugátēr Di(w)ós</i>, and Lith. <i>diẽvo duktė˜ (dukrýtė)</i>. In the RV, the deified ‘dawn’ [330], <i>Uṣas</i>, is usually addressed in this manner, and in Greek it is used, among others, of Aphrodite, who is <i>pʰilommeidḗs</i> ‘having a lovely smile’ (Hom.+), similar to Uṣas, who is frequently connected with the root <i>smi</i>- ‘smile’ (cf. Dunkel 1991). Other suspected members of the PIE pantheon − with decreasing degrees of certainty, as either no exact correspondences are attested or the reconstruction is formally problematic − are the ‘divine twins’ (: Skt. <i>divó nápātā</i>, Gk. <i>Di[w]óskouroi</i>, Latv. <i>Dieva dēli</i> ‘sons of heaven’ [usually twins], the Greek <i>Kastor</i> and <i>Poludeukēs</i>, borrowed into Latin as <i>Castor</i> and <i>Pollux</i>, who are called <i>Sōtēre</i> ‘the two saviours’ in Greek, corresponding to the Vedic <i>Nāsatyā</i> ‘saviours’), the god of the sea (: Lat. <i>Neptūnus</i>, OIr. <i>Nechtan</i>, Skt. <i>Apā́m Nápāt</i> ‘grandson of the waters’), a divine shepherd (: Gk. <i>Pā́ōn</i>, Skt. <i>Pū́ṣan</i>-, cf. Oettinger 2000), a smith (: Lat. <i>Vulcānus</i>, Osset. <i>Wergon</i>; cf. Vedic <i>ulkā´</i> ‘fire, flame’), and a physician (: Gk. <i>Apóllōn</i>, Skt. <i>Rudrá</i>-, cf. Oberlies 2000).

## 8. PIE poetic language (“Dichtersprache”)

Many poetic expressions (syntagms, metaphors, etc.) attested in the daughter languages are derived from PIE, the most famous one being probably *<i>léu̯ os ń̥dʰgᵘ̯ʰitom</i> ‘imperishable fame’ (: Gk. <i>klé[w]os ápʰtʰiton</i>, Skt. <i>śrávas ákṣitam</i>). The poet himself is one who ‘forges’ the words (*<i>tekt</i>-: Av. <i>vačas</i>-<i>tašti</i>- ‘the forging of words’, RV 5.2.11 <i>etáṃ stómaṃ</i> … <i>ráthaṃ ná atakṣam</i> ‘I have crafted this song like a chariot’, Pi. <i>P</i>. 3.113 <i>epéōn … téktones</i> ‘the forgers of words’), ‘weaves’ them into a ‘garment’ (: *<i>teks</i>-, cf. Lat. <i>textus</i>) or ‘harnesses’ his song of praise like a chariot (: Pi. <i>Nem</i>. 1.7 <i>enkṓmion zeũksai mélos</i>, RV 10.13.1 <i>yujé</i> … <i>bráhma</i>), cf. Schmitt (1967), Schmitt (1968), Meid (1978), Watkins (1995), and West (2007).

## 9. Vocabulary list

The material from 27–207 is arranged according to the Swadesh list. Only a selection of the material related to each root is given. Not all possible roots and reconstructions are listed here, for more material cf. IEW, LIV, and NIL, and, as onomasiological collections, Buck (1949) and EIEC.

27. ‘big, large’: *<i>meg̑-h₂-</i> (: Gk. <i>mégas</i>, Arm. <i>mec</i>, Skt. <i>máhi</i>-, Av. <i>maz</i>-; *<i>meg̑h₂-i</i>-: Hitt. <i>mēkki</i>-; [*<i>m̥g̑h₂</i>-<i>no</i>-] Lat. <i>magnus</i>; [*<i>meg̑h₂</i>-<i>lo</i>-] Goth. <i>mikils</i>, ON <i>mikill</i>, OE <i>micel</i>, OHG <i>mihhil</i>; Toch. A <i>māk</i>, B <i>māka</i> ‘many’, Alb. <i>madh</i> ‘large’, OIr. <i>maige</i>). IEW 708 f., EIEC 344, NIL 468 ff.

28. ‘long’: *<i>dl̥h₁gʰ</i>-<i>o</i>- (: Skt. <i>dīrghá</i>-, OAv. <i>darǝga</i>-, OCS <i>dъlgъ</i>, Lith. <i>ìlgas</i>. Gk. <i>en</i><i>delekʰē´s</i> ‘long-lasting, uninterrupted’ with full grade I [*<i>delh₁gʰ</i>-<i>es</i>-], <i>dolikʰó</i>- ‘long’ and Hitt. <i>daluki</i>- with different suffixes [*<i>dolh₁</i>-<i>i</i>-<i>gʰo</i>-, *<i>dolh₁</i>-<i>u</i>-<i>gʰo</i>-?] beside *<i>dlh₁</i>-<i>oN</i>-<i>gʰo</i>-in Lat. <i>longus</i>, Germ. *<i>langa</i>- [Goth. <i>laggs</i>, ON <i>langr</i>, OHG <i>lanc</i>, etc.], MPers. <i>drang</i> ‘duration’). IEW 196 f.; *<i>meh₂</i>- (: YAv. <i>mas</i>- ‘big’, Toch. A <i>mok</i>, B <i>moko</i> ‘old’, Gk. <i>mẽkos</i> ‘length’, <i>makrós</i> ‘long, big’, Lat. <i>macer</i> ‘meagre’, OHG <i>magar</i> ‘id.’, Hitt. <i>makla</i>-<i>nt</i>- ‘meagre, thin’, OIr. <i>machtae</i> ‘big’). IEW 699, EIEC 357, 574, NIL 478 ff.; *<i>duh₂-</i>/ <i>du̯eh₂</i>-<i>ro</i>- (: Gk. <i>dērós</i>, Skt. <i>dūrá</i>-, Arm. <i>erkar</i>, Lat. <i>dūrus</i> ‘hard’ [if from ‘long-lasting’], cf. root noun ‘length’ in Gk. [adv.] <i>dḗn</i> ‘for a long time’, Hitt. <i>tuwa</i> [adv.] ‘far’).

29. ‘wide’: cf. ‘broad’ [275].

30. ‘thick’: *<i>tegu</i>- (: Hitt. <i>tagu</i>- ‘thick, swollen’, OIr. <i>tiug</i>, Welsh <i>tew</i>, OHG <i>dicki</i>, ON <i>þykkr</i>, OE <i>þicce</i>). IEW 1057; ‘dense, thick, fixed’. *<i>bʰeng̑ ʰ</i>- (: Hitt. <i>panku</i>- ‘all’, Skt. <i>bahú</i>- ‘much, dense’, Arm. <i>bazowm</i> ‘much’, Gk. <i>pakʰús</i> ‘dense, thick’; cf. Skt. <i>bam̐ háyate</i> ‘strengthens’, Av. <i>bązaiti</i> ‘fixes’). LIV 76, IEW 127 f., NIL 13 ff., EIEC 574.

31. ‘heavy’: *<i>gᵘ̯(e)rh₂</i>-<i>u</i>- (: Skt. <i>gurú</i>-, Gk. <i>barús</i>, Lat. <i>gravis</i>, Goth. <i>kaurus</i>, OIr. <i>bair</i>, Latv. <i>grũts</i>, Lat. (Sabellic loanword) <i>brūtus</i> < *<i>gᵘ̯</i>ruh₂-<i>to</i>-). IEW 476 f., EIEC 264. → ‘mountain’ [171].

32. ‘small’: *<i>men</i>-<i>u</i>-/-<i>u̯o</i>- (: OIr. <i>menb</i> ‘small, tiny’, Gk. <i>mánu</i> ‘small’, Arm. <i>manr</i>; Skt. <i>manāk</i> ‘a little, slightly’; *<i>mei̯</i>-: Gk. <i>meíōn</i> ‘less’, Lat. <i>nimis</i> ‘too much’ [*‘not less’], etc.). IEW 711, 728 f., EIEC 528 f.

33. ‘short’: *<i>mreg̑ ʰ</i>- (: Gk. <i>brakʰús</i>, YAv. <i>mǝrǝzu</i>-, Lat. <i>brevis</i>, OHG <i>murg</i>, Skt. <i>múhur</i> ‘immediately’). IEW 750 f., EIEC 515.

34. ‘narrow’: *<i>h₂emg̑ʰ</i>-<i>ú</i>- (: Skt. <i>am˙hú</i>-, Goth. <i>aggwus</i>, Arm. <i>anjowk</i>, OIr. <i>cum-ung</i>, OCS <i>o˛zъkъ</i>). LIV 264 f., IEW 42 f., NIL 301 ff., EIEC 391. From a verbal root ‘to cord up, make tight’ (: Gk. <i>ágkʰō</i>, Lat. <i>angere</i>, <i>-ō</i>, Av. °<i>āza</i>-).

35. ‘thin’: *<i>ten(H)</i>-<i>u</i>- (: Skt. <i>tanú</i>-, Gk. <i>tanaós</i> ‘thin, stretched out’ [*<i>tn̥(H)</i>-<i>u̯</i>-<i>o</i>-], <i>tanu°</i> [e.g., <i>tanú</i>-<i>pʰullos</i> ‘with thin leaves’], Lat. <i>tenuis</i>, OIr. <i>tanae</i>, OHG <i>dunni</i>, OCS <i>tьnъkъ</i>‘fine, soft’, Lith. <i>té˛vas</i>. Probably adj. built from verbal root *<i>ten(H)</i>- ‘stretch’). LIV 626, IEW 1065–1066, 1069, EIEC 574, NIL 694 ff.

36. ‘woman’: *<i>gᵘ̯en(h₂</i>)- (: probably Hitt. <i>kwinna</i>- [acc.sg.], cLuw. <i>wana</i>-, OIr. <i>bé</i> [n.], Skt. <i>jáni</i>-, <i>gnā´</i>-, Av. <i>jaini</i>-, <i>gǝnā</i>-, Arm. <i>kin</i>, Toch. A <i>śäm</i>,<i>̣</i> B <i>śana</i>, Gk. <i>gunḗ</i>, [Boeot.] <i>bánā</i>, Goth. <i>qino</i>, <i>qēns</i>, OCS <i>žena</i>, OPr. <i>genna</i>). IEW 473 f. EIEC 648, NIL 177 ff. For *<i>(h₁e)sor</i>- see 2.

37. ‘man’: *<i>h₂ner</i>- (: Gk. <i>anḗr</i>, Arm. <i>ayr</i>, phryg. <i>anar</i>, Skt. Av. <i>nar</i>-, Osc. <i>niir</i>, Lat. PN <i>Nerō</i>, Alb. <i>njerí</i>). IEW 765, EIEC 366, NIL 332 ff.; *<i>u̯iHro</i>- (: Skt. <i>vīrá</i>-, Av. <i>vīra</i>-, Lith. <i>výras</i>, Latv. <i>virs</i>, OPr. <i>wijrs</i>, Lat. <i>vir</i>, Umbr. <i>ueiro</i>, OIr. <i>fer</i>, Welsh <i>gwr</i>, ON <i>verr</i>, OE OHG <i>wer</i>, probably Gk. PN <i>Ἶros</i>; note the pun in <i>Od</i>. 18.73 <i>Ἶros Ἄīros</i> ‘Iros [i.e. the powerful one] [may soon be a powerless one]’). IEW 1178, EIEC 366 f., NIL 726 ff. → [291], [392], [396].

38. ‘human being’: in most languages designated as either ‘mortal’, *<i>mr̥ to</i>-, *<i>n̥k̑</i>-<i>u̯o</i>- → [109] or ‘earthling’, *<i>dʰg̑ ʰm</i>-<i>on</i>- → ‘earth’ [159], cf. 1.

39. ‘child’: *<i>putló</i>- (: Skt. <i>putrá</i>- ‘boy, child, young one’, Av. <i>puθra</i>-, Osc. <i>puklum</i> [acc.] ‘son’, Lat. <i>putillus</i>, <i>puer</i> ‘boy’; prob. related to Lat. <i>paucus</i> ‘little, few’ and Gk. <i>paĩs</i>/ <i>paũs</i> ‘boy, child’). IEW 842 f. *<i>tek̑no</i>- (: Gk. <i>téknon</i>, Germ. *<i>þegnaz</i> in ON <i>þegn</i> ‘free man’, OE <i>ðegn</i>, OS OHG <i>thegan</i> from *<i>tek̑-</i> ‘beget, bear’ [249]). IEW 1057, EIEC 106 f. → ‘suck’ [95].

40. ‘wife’: PIE does not seem to have had special terms for ‘wife’ and ‘husband’, cf. Benveniste (1969.1: 239 ff.).

41. ‘husband’: cf. [40].

42. ‘mother’: *<i>māter</i>- (: Skt. <i>mātár</i>-, Av. <i>mātar</i>-, Arm. <i>mayr</i>, Gk. <i>mḗtēr</i>, Lat. <i>māter</i>, OHG <i>muoter</i>, OIr. <i>máthair</i>, Lith. <i>mótė</i> ‘woman, wife’, <i>mótyna</i> ‘mother’, ORuss. <i>mati</i>, Alb. <i>motër</i> ‘sister’, Toch. A <i>mācar</i>, B <i>mācer</i>). IEW 700 f., EIEC 385 f., NIL 457 ff.

43. ‘father’: *<i>ph₂ter</i>- (: Gk. <i>patḗr</i>, Skt. <i>pitár</i>-, Av. <i>pitar</i>-, Goth. <i>fadar</i> [attested only once, otherwise replaced by <i>atta</i>], Toch. B <i>pācer</i>, A <i>pācar</i>, Arm. <i>hayr</i>, OIr. <i>athair</i>, Lat. <i>pater</i>). IEW 829, EIEC 194 f., NIL 554 ff.

44. ‘(wild) animal’: *<i>g̑ʰu̯er</i>- (: Gk. <i>tʰḗr</i>/<i>pʰḗr</i>, derivatives in Lat. <i>ferus</i> ‘wild [animal]’, Lith. <i>žverìs</i>). LIV 182, IEW 493, EIEC 22 ff.

45. ‘fish’: *<i>dʰg̑ ʰu(H)</i>- (: Gk. <i>ikʰtʰús</i>, Lith. <i>žuvìs</i>, OPr. <i>suckis</i>, Arm. <i>jowkn</i>). IEW 416; *<i>pei̯sk</i>- (: Lat. <i>piscis</i>, Goth. <i>fisks</i>). IEW 796, EIEC 204 f.

<!-- source-file: content/14_chapter08_12.xhtml -->

46. ‘bird’: *<i>h₂eu̯i</i>- (: Skt. <i>víḥ</i>, Av. <i>vīš</i>, Lat. <i>avis</i>, Arm. <i>haw</i>, Gk. <i>a[w]ietós</i> ‘eagle’, Alb. <i>vido</i> ‘dove’). IEW 86, EIEC 66 ff. → ‘egg’ [67].

47. ‘dog’: *<i>u̯ōn</i>, gen. *<i>unos</i> (: Hitt. <i>kuwaš</i> ‘dog-man’, Skt. <i>ś₍ᵤ₎vā´</i>, Av. <i>span</i>-, Gk. <i>kúōn</i>, Arm. <i>šown</i>, Lith. <i>šuõ</i>, Goth. <i>hunds</i> [<*<i>k̑u̯n̥to</i>-], OIr. <i>cú</i>, Toch. AB <i>ku</i>). IEW 632, EIEC 168, NIL 436 ff.

48. ‘louse, louse egg’: *<i>(s)knid</i>- (: MIr. <i>sned</i> ‘nit’, OE <i>hnitu</i>, OHG <i>[h]niz</i>, Gk. <i>kónis</i>, pl. <i>konídes</i>, Alb. [Geg] <i>thëni</i>; variant *<i>gʰnid</i>- in ON <i>gnit</i>, Russ. <i>gnída</i>, Latv. <i>gnîda</i>, *<i>gʰlid</i>-in Lith. <i>glìnda</i>, Lat. <i>lēns</i>, <i>lendis</i>). IEW 608; ‘louse’: *<i>lūs</i>- (: OHG OE <i>lūs</i>, ON <i>lús</i>, Welsh [pl.] <i>llau</i>, with tabooistic distortion Skt. <i>yūkā</i>, Lith. <i>liū˜lė</i>, <i>utė[lė]</i>, Russ. <i>voš</i>). IEW 692, EIEC 357.

49. ‘snake, dragon’: *<i>He</i>/<i>ogᵘ̯ʰi</i>- (: Gk. <i>ékʰis</i>, <i>ópʰis</i>, Arm. <i>iž</i>, Skt. <i>áhi</i>-, Av. <i>aži</i>- ‘snake, dragon’). ‘eel, snake’: *<i>h₂engᵘ̯ʰi</i>- (: Lat. <i>anguis</i> ‘snake’, <i>anguilla</i> ‘eel’, Gk. <i>égkʰelus</i> ‘eel’, other material that is possibly related: Lith. <i>ungurỹs</i> ‘eel’, Germ. *<i>æla</i>- ‘eel’ in OHG <i>āl</i>, OE <i>ǣl</i> etc., Hitt. <i>illuy</i>-<i>ankaš</i> with inversed order of the elements found in Lat. <i>angu-illa</i>, cf. Katz 1998). IEW 44, EIEC 529 f.

50. ’worm’: *<i>u̯erm</i>- (: Lat. <i>vermis</i> [*<i>u̯ermi</i>-], Gk. <i>hrómos</i> [*<i>u̯romo</i>-] ‘wood-worm’, Lith. <i>var˜mas</i> ‘insect, gnat’, ORuss. <i>vermie</i> ‘insects’, Goth. <i>waurms</i> [*<i>u̯r̥mo</i>-]). IEW 1152, EIEC 649 f.

51. ‘tree, wood’: *<i>dóru</i>, gen. *<i>dréu̯s</i> (: Skt. <i>dā´ru</i>, <i>dróḥ</i>), gen. *<i>doru̯os</i> (: Gk. <i>dóru</i>, <i>dourós</i> ‘stem, tree, spear’); Goth. <i>triu</i>, NE <i>tree</i>, Alb. <i>dru</i>, OIr. <i>daur</i> ‘oak’, Hitt. <i>daru</i>-, OCS <i>drěvo</i>. IEW 214 ff., EIEC 598 ff. Perhaps related to *<i>der</i>- ‘skin, flay’ → [115] as the trunk stripped of its twigs, foliage and/or bark.

52. ‘forest’: most probably PIE used derivatives of [51] of the type Gk. <i>drumós</i>, <i>drumá</i> ‘woods, forest’ (: Skt. <i>drumá</i>- ‘tree, plant’, Russ. <i>drom</i> ‘thicket’).

53. ‘stick’:? *<i>stobʰo</i>- (: Lith. <i>stãbas</i> ‘post, pillar, cramp’, Germ. *<i>staba</i>/<i>i</i>-, Goth. <i>stabeis</i> ‘elements, letters’), verbal root *<i>stebʰ</i>- ‘freeze, grow stiff’ (: Lith. <i>stembù</i>, <i>stèbti</i> ‘be amazed’), probably related to *<i>stembʰH-</i> ‘support’ (: Skt. <i>stabhnā´ti</i> ‘supports, fixes’, Toch B. <i>śama</i>, A <i>śäm</i> ‘stood’, Lith. <i>stembiù</i>, <i>stem˜bti</i> ‘resist’, Skt. <i>stambha</i>- ‘post, pillar’). LIV 588 f., 595, IEW 1012 f.

54. ‘fruit’:? *<i>kr̥po</i>- (: Gk. <i>karpós</i>) from *(<i>s</i>)<i>kerp</i>- ‘cut, pluck’ [307]. (EIEC 63).

55. ‘seed’:? *<i>seh₁</i>-<i>men</i>- is restricted to western languages (: Lat. <i>sēmen</i>, OHG <i>sāmo</i>, OPr. <i>semen</i>, OCS <i>sěmę</i>), → *<i>seh₁-</i> ‘sow’ [243]. IEW 889 f., EIEC 505.

56. ‘leaf’: *<i>bʰel</i>- (with different suffixes; Gk. <i>pʰúllon</i>, Lat. <i>folium</i> [*<i>bʰl̥</i>-<i>i̯o</i>-], Toch. A <i>pält</i>, MIr. <i>bileóc</i>, OHG <i>blat</i> [Germ. *<i>blada</i>-] etc.), perhaps related to *<i>bʰleh3-</i> ‘blossom’ (: OE <i>blōwan</i>, Lat. <i>flōs</i>, -<i>ōris</i> ‘flower’, OS <i>blōma</i> ‘flower’, OIr. <i>bláth</i>). IEW 122, EIEC 348.

57. ‘root’: *<i>u̯r̥Hd</i>- (: Lat. <i>rādīx</i>, Gk. <i>rʰā´diks</i> ‘branch’, <i>rʰíza</i> ‘root’, ON <i>rōt</i>, Goth. <i>waurts</i>, Welsh <i>gwraidd</i> ‘roots’, probably Toch. B <i>witsako</i> ‘root’). IEW 1167.

58. ‘bark (n.)’:?*<i>lou̯bo</i>- (: Lith. <i>luõbas</i>, Russ. <i>lub</i> ‘bast, bark’, Alb. <i>labë</i> ‘rind, bark, crust’; Lat. <i>liber</i> ‘bast; book’, ON <i>laupr</i> ‘basket’, OHG <i>louft</i> ‘bast, bark’). IEW 690, EIEC 50.

59. ‘flower’: Probably *<i>h₂endʰos</i>- (: Gk. <i>ántʰos</i> ‘sprout’, Arm. <i>and</i> ‘field’, Skt. <i>ándhas</i>-‘shoot, sprout’, Alb. <i>ëndë</i>). IEW 40 f., EIEC 207; different derivatives from *<i>bʰleh3-</i> in various languages, → ‘leaf’ [56].

60. ‘grass’: *<i>oi̯no</i>- (: Lith. <i>šiẽnas</i> ‘hay’, OCS <i>sěno</i> ‘hay, grass’, Gk. [Hsch.] <i>koiná</i>). IEW 610; *<i>u̯elt</i>- (: Welsh <i>gwellt</i>, OPr. <i>wolti</i> ‘blade of grass’, Lith. <i>váltis</i> ‘oat panicle’, Hitt. <i>wellu[u̯ant</i>-<i>]</i> ‘grass’). IEW 1139 f., EIEC 240.

61. ‘rope’: −

62. ‘skin’: *<i>tu̯ek</i>- (: Skt. <i>tvák</i>, <i>tvacas</i>-, Gk. <i>sákos</i> ‘shield’ [made of skin], Hitt. <i>twekkaš</i> ‘body, person, self’). IEW 1099; prob. *<i>(s)kuH</i>-<i>ti</i>/<i>o</i>- (: OHG <i>hūt</i> ‘hide’, Gk. <i>skũtos</i> ‘skin, hide, leather’, Toch. A <i>kāc</i> ‘skin’). IEW 952, EIEC 522.

63. ‘meat’: *<i>mēms(o)</i>- (: perhaps related to *<i>Hōmo</i>- ‘raw’ [271], or a compound *<i>me-h₁ems</i>- ‘in the middle of the back’ [<*<i>h₁oms</i>- ‘what is cut up’ > ‘back (of a slaughtered animal)’, *<i>h₁ems</i>- ‘flay, cut’, cf. Lat. <i>ēnsis</i> ‘sword’, Ved. <i>así</i>- ‘sword, slaughtering knife’] as analyzed by Pinault [ms.]; Skt. [acc.sg.] <i>mā´s</i>, <i>māṃsá</i>-, Arm. <i>mis</i>, Goth. <i>mimz</i>, OCS <i>męso</i>, Alb. <i>mish</i>, Toch. B <i>misa</i>, Lith. <i>mėsà</i>; derivative *<i>mēms</i>-<i>ro</i>- ‘part of meat, body part’ in Gk. <i>mērós</i> ‘thigh, thigh bone’, Lat. <i>membrum</i> ‘body part, part’, OIr. <i>mír</i> ‘bit, portion’). IEW 725, EIEC 374, NIL 486 ff.; → ‘shoulder’ [283]; ‘raw meat, blood’: *<i>kreu̯h₂</i>- (‘meat’: Gk. <i>kréas</i>, Skt. <i>kravíṣ</i>-; ‘blood’: Lith. <i>kraũjas</i>, Lat. <i>cruor</i>, OCS <i>krъvь</i>, MIr. <i>crú</i>; Germ. adj. ‘raw’ in OHG <i>[h]rō</i>, OS <i>hrā</i>, OE <i>hrēaw</i>, ON <i>hrár</i>). IEW 621 f., EIEC 71, NIL 444 ff.

64. ‘blood’: *<i>h₁esh₂</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- (: Hitt. <i>ēšḫar</i>, gen. <i>ēšnaš</i>, Skt. <i>ásr̥k</i>, gen. <i>asnás</i>, Toch. A <i>ysār</i>, B <i>yasar</i>, Gk. <i>éar</i>, Latv. <i>asins</i>, OLat. <i>as[s]er</i>, Arm. <i>ar</i>-<i>iwn</i>). IEW 343, EIEC 71.

65. ‘bone’: *<i>h₃est</i>- (: Skt. <i>ásthi</i>, gen. <i>asthnáḥ</i>, Av. <i>ast</i>-, <i>asti</i>-, Gk. <i>ostéon</i>, Alb. <i>asht</i>, <i>ashte¨</i>, Lat. <i>os</i>, gen. <i>ossis</i>, Hitt. <i>ḫastāi</i>-). IEW 783, EIEC 77.

66. ‘fat’: *<i>piHu̯en</i>/<i>u̯er</i>- (: Gk. <i>pī́ōn</i>, [f.] <i>pī́eira</i>, Skt. <i>pī́van</i>-, [f.] <i>pī́varī</i>, from a verbal root *<i>pei̯H</i>- ‘swell’, Skt. <i>páyate</i> ‘swells’, <i>pínvati</i> ‘makes swell’, Lith. <i>pyjù</i>, <i>pýti</i> ‘produce milk’). IEW 793 f., → ‘swell’ [146]; *<i>selp</i>- (: Gk. <i>élpos</i> ‘fat, olive oil’ [Hsch.], Skt. <i>sarpíṣ</i>- ‘ghee’, Toch. A <i>ṣälyp</i>, B <i>ṣalype</i> ‘oil, fat, unguent’, Alb. <i>gjalpë</i> ‘butter’, OHG <i>salb</i> ‘unguent’). IEW 901, EIEC 194, NIL 612 f.

67. ‘egg’: *<i>Hōu̯io</i>- (: Gk. <i>ōión</i>, Lat. <i>ōvum</i>, Welsh <i>wy</i>, Alb. <i>voe</i>, OCS <i>ajьce</i>, OHG <i>ei</i>, OE <i>ǣ[i]g</i>). IEW 783 f., EIEC 176. Probably a derivative of → ‘bird’ [46].

68. ‘horn’: *<i>erh₂-</i> and derivatives (: Lat. <i>cornū</i>, Skt. <i>śŕ̥ṅga</i>-, Gk. <i>kéras</i>, Germ. *<i>hurna</i>-in Goth. <i>haurn</i>, OHG OE ON <i>horn</i>). IEW 574 ff., EIEC 272. → ‘head’ [72], → ‘hornet’ [356].

69. ‘tail’:? *<i>u̯olo</i>- (: Lith. <i>vãlas</i>, pl. <i>valaĩ</i> ‘horse-tail’, Skt. <i>vā´ra</i>- ‘horse-tail, hair of a horse’s tail’), from *<i>u̯el</i>- ‘turn’ (: Arm. <i>gelowm</i>, Lat. <i>volvere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, Alb. <i>vjell</i> ‘vomits’, Gk. <i>eiléō</i>). LIV 675, IEW 1140 ff.; *<i>puk</i>-<i>o</i>-/-<i>s(k̑)o</i>- (: Skt. <i>púccha</i>- ‘tail’, Germ. *<i>fuh[s]a</i>-‘fox’, Goth. <i>fauhō</i>, OE <i>fox</i>, OHG <i>fuhs</i> [named after its characteristic tail], Toch. B <i>pako</i>* ‘tail, cowrie’). IEW 849, EIEC 563.

70. ‘feather’: cf. words for ‘wing’ [84].

71. ‘hair’: *<i>peu̯mos</i>- (: Skt. <i>púmām̐s</i>- ‘man; male being’, Lat. <i>pūbēs</i>, <i>puber</i>- [*<i>peu̯m</i>-<i>ro</i>-] ‘male, coming of age; pubic hair’, Šughnī <i>pūm</i> ‘down, fluff’). IEW −, cf. Mayrhofer 2006.2: 144. For further possible etyma cf. EIEC 251 ff.

72. ‘head’: *<i>erh₂-</i> and derivatives (: Skt. <i>śíras</i>-, Av. <i>sarah</i>-, Gk. <i>kárā</i>, Lat. <i>cerebrum</i> ‘brain’, OHG <i>hirni</i> ‘brain’), cf. Nussbaum 1986: 158 ff. IEW 574 ff., EIEC 260 f. → ‘horn’ [68].

73. ‘ear’: *<i>h₂ou̯s</i>-<i>os</i>- (: Gk. <i>oús</i>, gen. <i>ōtós</i>, OIr. <i>áu</i>, OCS <i>uxo</i>, Av. <i>uši</i>-, Lat. <i>auris</i>, Lith. <i>ausìs</i>, Arm. <i>ownkn</i>). IEW 785, EIEC 173, NIL 339 ff.

74. ‘eye’: *<i>h₃ekᵘ̯</i>- (: Lat. <i>oculus</i>, Lith. <i>akìs</i>, OCS <i>oko</i>, Goth. <i>augo</i>, Gk. <i>ōps</i>*, <i>óps</i>, <i>opʰtʰalmós</i>, <i>ómma</i>, Arm. <i>akn</i>, Skt. <i>ákṣi</i>-). LIV 297 f., IEW 775 ff., NIL 370 ff., EIEC 188.

75. ‘nose, nostril(s)’: *<i>nas</i>- (: Skt. [du.] <i>nā´sā</i>, Av. <i>nāh</i>-, <i>nā̊ŋhan</i>-, Lat. <i>nāris</i>, usually pl. -<i>ēs</i>, <i>nāsum</i>, <i>nās[s]us</i>, OHG <i>nasa</i>, OE <i>nasu</i>, OPr. <i>nozy</i>, Lith. <i>nósis</i>, OCS <i>nozdri</i> ‘nostrils’, <i>nоsъ</i>). IEW 755, EIEC 395.

76. ‘mouth’: *<i>h₃oh₁</i>-<i>(e)s</i>- (: Hitt. <i>aiš</i>, gen. <i>iššāš</i>, Ved. <i>ās</i>-, Lat. <i>ōs</i>, <i>ōris</i>, OIr. <i>á</i>, Lith. <i>úostas</i> ‘mouth of a river, harbor’). IEW 784 f., EIEC 387, NIL 387 ff.

77. ‘tooth’: *<i>h₁d(o)nt</i>- (: Skt. <i>dant</i>-, Gk. <i>ódous</i>, -<i>ontos</i>, Lat. <i>dens</i>, <i>dentis</i>, Goth. <i>tunþus</i>, Arm. <i>atamn</i>, OIr. <i>dét</i>). LIV 230 f., IEW 287 ff., EIEC 594. → ‘bite, eat’ [93].

78. ‘tongue’: *<i>d⁽ʰ⁾(e)ng̑ʰ</i>-<i>uH</i>- (: Skt. <i>jihvā´</i>, Av. <i>hizvā</i>, Arm. <i>lezow</i>, OLat. <i>dingua</i>, Lat. <i>lingua</i>, Osc. <i>fangvam</i>, OIr. <i>teng</i>, Goth. <i>tuggō</i>, Lith. <i>liežùvis</i>, OCS <i>językъ</i>, Toch. A <i>käntu</i>, B <i>käntwo</i> [with metathesis from *<i>tanku̯a</i>]). IEW 223, EIEC 594.

79. ‘fingernail’: *<i>h₃nogᵘ̯ʰ</i>- (: Gk. <i>ónuks</i>, -<i>ukʰos</i>, Lat. <i>unguis</i>, OIr. <i>ingen</i>, OHG <i>nagal</i>, Lith. <i>nãgas</i> ‘nail’, <i>nagà</i> ‘hoof’, OCS <i>noga</i> ‘foot’, Skt. <i>án˙ghri</i>- ‘foot’). IEW 780, EIEC 389.

80. ‘foot’: *<i>ped</i>-/<i>pod</i>- (: Gk. <i>poús</i>, <i>podós</i>, Skt. <i>pād</i>-, Lat. <i>pēs</i>, <i>pedis</i>, Goth. <i>fotus</i>, Toch. A <i>pe</i>, B <i>payye</i>, Luw. <i>pāta</i>-, Arm. <i>otn</i>, OIr. <i>ís</i> ‘beneath’ < [loc.] *<i>pēd</i>-<i>su</i> ‘at the feet’, Alb. <i>(për-)posh</i> ‘below, underneath’ < *<i>pēd-su</i>/<i>si</i>). LIV 458, IEW 790 ff., NIL 526 ff., EIEC 280 f.

81. ‘leg’:? *<i>k̑rūs</i>- (: Lat. <i>crūs</i>, -<i>ris</i> ‘shank, leg’, Arm. <i>srownk‘</i> ‘shin, calf’(?)). IEW 624;? *<i>sekᵘ̯t</i>- (: Skt. <i>sákthi</i>-, Av. <i>haxti</i>- ‘thigh’, prob. Toch. B <i>ckāckai</i> ‘shank’, Hitt. <i>šakut</i>-‘upper leg’); *<i>konHmo</i>- (: OIr. <i>cnáim</i> ‘leg’, OE <i>hamm</i> ‘ham’, Gk. <i>knḗmē</i> ‘tibia, spoke of a wheel’). EIEC 349.

82. ‘knee’: *<i>g̑e</i>/<i>on</i>-<i>u</i>- (: Skt. <i>jā´nu</i>, Av. [acc.] <i>žnūm</i>, Gk. <i>gónu</i>, Arm. <i>cownr</i>, Lat. <i>genū</i>, -<i>ūs</i>, Goth. <i>kniu</i>, Toch. A <i>kanweṃ</i>, В <i>kenīne</i> [du.], Hitt. <i>genu</i>). IEW 381, EIEC 336.

83. ‘hand’: *<i>g̑ʰes</i>-<i>r</i>- (: Hitt. <i>kiššar</i>-, Gk. <i>kʰeír</i>, Arm. <i>jer̄n</i>, Alb. <i>dorë</i>, Toch. A <i>tsar</i>, B <i>ṣar</i>), *<i>g̑ʰes</i>-<i>to</i>- (: Skt. <i>hásta</i>-, Av. <i>zasta</i>-, OP <i>dasta</i>-, probably Lat. <i>hostus</i> ‘yield of an olive-tree’, cf. de Vaan 2008: 292). IEW 446 f., EIEC 254, NIL 170 ff.

84. ‘wing’: *<i>pet(H)</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- (: Hitt. <i>pattar</i>, Skt. <i>patan˙gá</i>- ‘flying; bird’, Welsh <i>adain</i>/-<i>en</i>, Lat. <i>penna</i> ‘feather, wing’ [*<i>petnā</i>], Gk. <i>pterón</i>, CSl. <i>pero</i>, OE <i>feder</i>, OHG <i>fedara</i>, etc.), from *<i>peth₁</i>- ‘fly, fall’ [120] or *<i>peth₂</i>- ‘spread wings’ [120]. LIV 477 ff., IEW 824 ff., EIEC 646.

85. ‘belly’: *<i>Hudero</i>-? (: Skt. <i>udára</i>- ‘belly’, Gk. <i>húderos</i> ‘dropsy’, <i>hóderos</i>· <i>gastḗr</i> [Hsch.], Lat. <i>uterus</i>, Lith. <i>vė́daras</i> ‘entrails’, Latv. <i>vêders</i>/-<i>ars</i> ‘belly, stomach’). IEW 1104 f.

86. ‘guts’: *<i>h₁enter-o</i>- (: Gk. <i>éntera</i>, Arm. <i>ǝnderk‘</i>, ON <i>iðrar</i>; Skt. <i>āntrá</i>-), from adj. *<i>h₁en</i>-<i>tero</i>- ‘inner, being inside’ (: Skt. <i>ántara</i>-, Lat. [comp.] <i>interior</i>) from *<i>h₁en(i)</i> ‘in’. IEW 313. More divergent meanings in *<i>g̑ ʰr̥H(d</i>/<i>n)</i>- (: Gk. <i>kʰordḗ</i> ‘bowel’, Lith. <i>žarnà</i>, ON <i>gorn</i>, Lat. <i>haru</i>-<i>spex</i> ‘diviner [from the entrails of victims]’, Skt. <i>híra</i>- ‘rope’, <i>hirā´</i>-‘vein’). IEW 443, EIEC 179 f.

87. ‘neck’: *<i>mon</i>-<i>o</i>- (: Skt. <i>mányā</i>, Av. <i>manaoθrī</i>, Lat. <i>monīle</i> ‘necklace’, OIr. <i>muin</i>- in <i>muin</i>-<i>torc</i> ‘necklace’, <i>muinél</i> ‘throat, neck’, Welsh <i>mwn</i>, <i>mwnwgl</i> ‘neck, throat’; ON <i>men</i>, OE <i>mene</i>, OHG <i>menni</i> ‘necklace’). IEW 747; *<i>gᵘ̯</i>riHu̯eh₂- (: Skt. <i>grīvā´</i>-, Av. <i>grīu-uā</i>-, Russ. <i>gríva</i> ‘mane, ridge’, Latv. <i>grīva</i> ‘mouth of a river’, probably Gk. <i>dérē</i>). IEW 474 f., EIEC 391 f.

88. ‘back’:? *<i>(s)kuH</i>-<i>lo</i>- (: Skt. <i>kū́la</i>- ‘slope, rear of an army’, OIr. <i>cúl</i> ‘back’, Lat. <i>cūlus</i> ‘rear-end’). IEW 951, EIEC 42. Perhaps *<i>h₁e</i>/<i>oms</i>- → ‘meat’ [63].

89. ‘breast’: *<i>psten</i>- (: OHG <i>spunna</i>, MHG <i>spen</i>, <i>spun[n]e</i>, OE <i>spanu</i>, etc. ‘breast’, Lith. <i>spenỹs</i>, OIr. <i>sine</i> ‘tit’, Skt. <i>stána</i>-, Av. <i>fštana</i>-, Gk. <i>stẽnion</i> [Hsch.]). IEW 990. ‘breast, udder’: *<i>Hou̯Hdʰ-r</i>/<i>n-</i> (: Skt. <i>ū´dhar</i>-, Gk. <i>oũtʰar</i>, Lat. <i>ūber</i>, MHG <i>ūter</i>, Lith. <i>udróti</i> ‘be heavy with young’). IEW 347, EIEC 81.

90. ‘heart’: *<i>erd</i>- (: Hitt. <i>kẽr</i>, <i>kard</i>-, Lat. <i>cor</i>, <i>cordis</i>, Gk. <i>kẽr</i>, <i>kardía</i>/<i>kradíē</i>, Lith. <i>širdìs</i>, Arm. <i>sirt</i>, Skt. <i>hr̥d</i>- with changed anlaut, OCS <i>srъdьce</i>, Goth. <i>hairto</i>, OIr. <i>cride</i>, Toch. <i>mäl</i>-<i>kärteṃ</i> ‘magnanimous’). IEW 579 f., EIEC 262 f., NIL 417 ff. → ‘put the heart, believe, trust’ [362].

91. ‘liver’: *<i>i̯e</i>/<i>okᵘ̯</i>-<i>r</i>/<i>n</i>- (: Skt. <i>yákr̥</i>-<i>t</i>, gen. <i>yaknás</i>, Lat. <i>iecur</i>/<i>iocur</i>, gen. <i>iocineris</i>/<i>iocinoris</i>, Gk. <i>hḗpar</i>, -<i>atos</i>, Lith. [dial.] <i>jẽknos</i>, MIr. <i>iuchair</i> ‘roe’, ORuss. <i>ikra</i> ‘id.’; doubtful Arm. <i>leard</i> and Germ. *<i>liƀrō</i>, NE <i>liver</i>, NHG <i>Leber</i>, perhaps from a word for ‘fat’, cf. Gk. <i>liparós</i> ‘fat’). IEW 504, EIEC 356, NIL 392 ff.

92. ‘drink’: *<i>peh₃(i̯)</i>- (: Skt. <i>píbati</i>, Lat. <i>bibere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, OIr. <i>ibid</i>, Arm. <i>əmpem</i>, Gk. <i>pī́nō</i>, OCS <i>pijǫ</i>, <i>piti</i>, Hitt. <i>pāši</i> ‘swallows’, OPr. <i>poieiti</i> [ipv.] ‘drink!’); *<i>h₁egᵘ̯ʰ</i>- (: Hitt. <i>ekuzi</i>, Toch. B <i>yokäṃ</i>, A <i>yoktsi</i> ‘drinks, to drink’, cf. Lat. <i>ēbrius</i> ‘drunk’). LIV 462 f., IEW 839 f., EIEC 175 f.

93. ‘eat, bite’: *<i>h₁ed</i>- (: Skt. <i>átti</i>, <i>adánti</i>, Hitt. <i>ēdmi</i>, <i>adanzi</i>, Gk. [fut.] <i>édomai</i>, Lat. <i>edere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, OIr. <i>ithid</i>, Goth. <i>itan</i>, OLith. <i>ė́mi</i>, OCS <i>jamь</i>, <i>jasti</i>, Arm. <i>owtem</i>). LIV 230 f., IEW 287 ff., NIL 208 ff, EIEC 175 f. → ‘tooth’ [77].

94. ‘bite’: *<i>denk̑</i>- (: Skt. <i>dáśati</i>, Gk. <i>dáknō</i>, ON <i>tengja</i> ‘connect’; cf. Gk. <i>ódaks</i> ‘with the teeth’, Skt. <i>daṃśman</i>- ‘bit’ etc.). LIV 117 f., IEW 201, NIL 82 f., EIEC 68, → [93].

95. ‘suck’: *<i>dʰeh1(i̯)</i>- (: cLuw. [ptc.] <i>titaimi</i>- ‘fed’, Lyc. <i>tideimi</i> ‘child’, Skt. <i>dhinoti</i> ‘feeds’, OIr. <i>denait</i> [3pl.] ‘suck’, MWelsh <i>dyn</i>-, Gk. [aor.] <i>tʰē</i>-<i>s</i>-, Arm. <i>diem</i>, OHG <i>tāen</i>*, Latv. <i>dêju</i>, <i>dêt</i>, Goth. <i>daddjan</i>, OCS <i>dojǫ</i>, <i>dojiti</i> ‘suckle’). LIV 138 f., IEW 241 <i>k̑</i>f.; *<i>seu̯</i>-(: Lat. <i>sūgere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, ON <i>súga</i>, OCS <i>sъsǫ</i>, <i>sъsati</i>, Lith. <i>sunkiù</i>, <i>suñkti</i> ‘press, filter’). LIV 539 f., IEW 912 f., EIEC 556.

96. ‘spit’: *<i>spti̯eu̯H</i>- (: Gk. <i>ptū´ō</i>, Skt. <i>ní ṣṭhīvati</i>, Goth. <i>speiwan</i>, Lat. <i>spuere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, Lith. <i>spiáuju</i>, <i>spiáuti</i>, OCS <i>pljujǫ</i>, <i>pljьvati</i>). LIV 583 f., IEW 999 f., EIEC 538.

97. ‘vomit’: *<i>u̯emh₁</i>- (: Gk. <i>eméō</i>, Lat. <i>vomere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, Skt. <i>vámiti</i>, Lith. <i>vemiù</i>, <i>vémti</i>). LIV 680, IEW 1146, EIEC 538.

98. ‘blow’: *<i>h₂u̯eh₁</i>- (: Skt. <i>vā´ti</i>, YAv. <i>vāiti</i>, Gk. <i>áēsi</i>, Goth. <i>waian</i>, OCS <i>vějati</i>). LIV 287, IEW 81–84, EIEC 71 f. → ‘wind’ [163].

99. ‘breathe’: *<i>h₂enh₁</i>- (: Skt. <i>ániti</i>, OIr. -<i>ana</i> ‘waits’, Goth. -<i>anan</i>*, Toch. B <i>anāsṣ̣äṃ</i>, Gk. <i>ánemos</i> ‘wind’, Lat. <i>animus</i>/<i>-a</i> ‘spirit, soul, self’). LIV 267 f., IEW 38 f., NIL 307 ff., EIEC 82. → ‘wind’ [163], → ‘smoke’ [166].

100. ‘laugh’: The daughter languages usually use onomatopoetic forms such as Gk. <i>ka(n)kʰázō</i>, Arm. <i>xaxank‘</i> ‘laughter’, Skt. <i>kakhati</i>, Lat. <i>cachinnāre</i>, -<i>ō</i>, OCS <i>xoxotati</i> (virtually from a root **<i>kʰakʰ</i>-), Goth. <i>hlahjan</i> (**<i>klak</i>-). A root attested only in Greek and Armenian is *<i>g̑el(h₂)</i>- ‘laugh/shine’ (: Gk. <i>gélōs</i> ‘laughter’, Arm. <i>całr</i> ‘id.’, Gk. <i>geláō</i> ‘I laugh’, Arm. <i>cicałim</i>, Gk. <i>galḗnē</i> ‘calm [*laughing] <b>/</b> shining sea’< *<i>g̑elasnā</i>, cf. <i>glaukós</i> ‘bright; bluish green, grey’, <i>glaússei</i>· <i>lámpei</i> ‘shines’ [Hsch.]). LIV 162, IEW 366, EIEC 344 f.

124. ‘sit’: *<i>sed</i>- ‘sit down’ (: Skt. <i>sad</i>-, gr. <i>hízomai</i>, Lat. <i>sedēre</i>, -<i>eō</i>, Goth. <i>sitan</i>, Arm. <i>nstim</i>, OIr. -<i>said</i>, <i>sedait</i>, Lith. <i>sė́du</i>, inf. <i>sė́sti</i>, OCS <i>sędǫ</i>, inf. <i>sěsti</i>). LIV 513 f., IEW 785 f., 884 ff., NIL 590 ff.; ‘sit’ *<i>h₁ēs</i>- (state) (: Skt. <i>ā´ste</i>, Av. 3pl. <i>ā̊ŋhāire</i>, Gk. <i>hē´stai</i>, Hitt. <i>ēša</i>). 126. ‘turn’: *<i>kᵘ̯</i>elh₁- (: [aor.] Gk. <i>épleto</i>, Arm. <i>ełew</i>, Alb. LIV 392 f., IEW 631; *<i>terkᵘ̯</i>- (: Hitt. <i>tarukzi</i>, Lat. <i>torquēre</i>, -<i>eō</i>, Alb. <i>tjerr</i> ‘spin’, Toch. B [ptc.] <i>tetarku</i>). 127. ‘fall’: ‘step, fall’: *<i>ped</i>- (: Skt. <i>pádyate</i> ‘steps, falls, goes’, YAv. [subj.] <i>paiδiiāite</i>, ON <i>fat</i> ‘found out’, OCS <i>padǫ</i>, <i>pasti</i> ‘fall’ → ‘foot’ [80]). LIV 194, IEW 437 f.; <i>h₁ep</i>-(: Hitt. <i>ēpzi</i>, OLat. <i>apere</i>, -<i>iō</i> ‘bind’, Alb. <i>[j]ep</i> ‘gives’, Skt. <i>ā´pa</i>, YAv. <i>āpa</i> ‘has reached’). 129. ‘hold’: *<i>dʰer</i>- (?) (: Skt. <i>dhāráyati</i> ‘holds, supports’, Av. <i>dāraiiat̰</i>, probably Lat. <i>firmus</i> ‘solid, firm’). LIV 145, IEW 252 f.; *<i>skebʰH</i>- (: Skt. <i>skabhnā´ti</i> ‘supports’, Lat. <i>scamnum</i> ‘stool, bench’). LIV 549, IEW 916. A stative or perfect formation of a verb ‘take, seize’ would have meant ‘have taken’ > ‘hold, have’ in PIE like Lat. <i>habēre</i>, -<i>eō</i> ‘have, possess, hold’ (: OIr. <i>-gaib</i> ‘takes’). EIEC 270.

130. ‘squeeze’: *<i>merh₂-</i> ‘take (with force), crunch’ (: Skt. <i>mr̥ṇā´ti</i> ‘crunches’, Gk. <i>márnamai</i> ‘I fight’, Alb. <i>merr</i> ‘takes’, ON <i>merja</i> ‘beat, destroy’, Hitt. <i>marritta</i> ‘is shredded’). LIV 438, IEW 730 f.; *<i>bʰrekᵘ̯</i>- (: Lat. <i>farciō</i>, -<i>īre</i> ‘cram’, Gk. <i>pʰrássō</i>/-<i>ttō</i> ‘I fence in’). LIV 93 f., IEW 110 f.

131. ‘rub’: *<i>terh₁</i>- ‘rub, drill’ (: Gk. <i>teírō</i>, Lat. <i>terere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, Lith. <i>tiriù</i>, <i>tìrti</i> ‘inquire, get to know’, OCS <i>tьrjǫ</i>, <i>trъti</i>), cf. LIV 632, IEW 1071 f.; *<i>g⁽ᵘ̯⁾ʰrend</i>(: Lat. <i>frendere</i>, -<i>ō</i> ‘crunch’, Lith. <i>gréndu</i>, <i>grę́sti</i> ‘scratch, rub’); cf. also *<i>g̑erh₂</i>- ‘rub, make old’ [252]. EIEC 490.

LIV 438, IEW 692; *<i>neigᵘ̯</i>- (: Gk. <i>nízō</i>, OIr. -<i>nig</i>, Skt. <i>nenikté</i>, YAv. <i>naēnižaiti</i>). LIV 450, IEW 761, NIL 519 f., EIEC 108 f.

133. ‘wipe’: *<i>h₂merg̑</i>- (: Skt. <i>mā´rṣṭi</i>, 3pl. <i>mr̥jánti</i> ‘wipes, cleanses’, YAv. <i>marǝzaiti</i> ‘touches’, Gk. <i>amérgō</i> ‘I pluck, harvest’, [aor.] Hom. <i>omorxámenos</i> ‘wiping [tears]’). LIV 280 f., IEW 738, EIEC 646.

134. ‘pull’: *<i>deu̯k</i>- (: Lat. <i>dūcere</i>, -<i>ō</i>, Goth. <i>tiuhan</i>, Alb. <i>n-duk</i>, MWelsh <i>dwc</i> ‘brings’, Oss. <i>duc</i>-/<i>doc</i>- ‘milk’, Toch. LIV 601, IEW 1033 f.; EIEC 471 with more proposals.

136. ‘throw’: *<i>Hi̯eh₁</i>- (: Hitt. <i>piyezzi</i> ‘sends’, Gk. <i>híēmi</i> ‘I throw, send’, Lat. <i>iacere</i>, -<i>iō</i> ‘throw’). LIV 225, IEW 502, EIEC 581.

137. ‘tie, bind’: *<i>bʰendʰ</i>- (: Goth. <i>bindan</i>, Skt. <i>badhnā´ti</i>, Av. <i>baṇdaiieiti</i>). LIV 75, IEW 127; *<i>Hnedʰ</i>- (: Skt. <i>náhyati</i>, OIr. -<i>naisc</i>). LIV 227, IEW 758; *<i>lei̯g</i>- (: Alb. <i>lidh</i>, Lat. <i>ligāre</i>, -<i>ō</i>, MLG <i>līk</i> ‘bond’).
